in January/February 1984, comprising D.M. Leeuwrik, R. Anson, and A. Youssef
(IDA), and J. Christopher, J. Waterhouse, and V. Resler (Consultants). Part-
time mission members were T.H. Yoon, S. Baier, G. Le Moigne, and G. Greenwood
(IDA). The appraisal was conducted jointly with missions from the Asian
Development Bank, the Overseas Development Administration (United Kingdom)
and the Canadian International Development Agency. H. Akhavan, A. Brooke
and M. Ahmad assisted with analytical and computational work. Ms. S. Blinco
provided editorial assistance.

PAKISTAN
LEFT BANK OUTFALL DRAIN STAGE I PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUNMARY
Borrower            : Islamic Republic of Pakistan (GOP)
Beneficiary         : Government of Sind (GOSind)
Amount              : IDA Credit:  SDR 147.6 million (M) (US$150 M equivalent)
Terms               : Standard
Re-Lendins Terms   : External funds received by GOP would be made available
to GOSind and the Water and Power Development Authority
of Pakistan (WAPDA) through budgetary allocations in
accordance with normal GOP procedures. GOP would
bear the foreign exchange risk.
Proiect Description:  The project, the first stage of a major drainage program
in Sind, would be implemented over eight years. It would
provide for the integrated development of irrigation
and drainage in a portion of the perennial command
of Sukkur Barrage on the left bank of the Indus River.
Principal project components would include an outfall
for saline drainage effluent to the Arabian Sea, phased
construction of three drainage subareas, remodelling
of the Nara/Jamrao Canal system and construction of
Chotiari Reservoir, and watercourse improvement.
Technical assistance would be provided in the
preparation of detailed designs and tender documents
and the planning and supervision of construction.
The project would cover 516,000 hectares (ha) of
cultivable command area (CCA) in three subareas-
Nawabshah, Sanghar, and Mirpurkhas. It would be the
first major investment in subsurface drainage of
saline groundwater in Sind since the Khairpur II
project was undertaken in 1976. It also would be
the first such project to dispose of saline drainage
effluent completely outside the area serviced. Both
rural and urban areas would benefit from surface drains
extending to the subareas. Drainage tubewells (TWs)
and subsurface tile drains would be provided for
drainage relief. In addition, horizontal interceptor
drains and scavenger TW8 would be installed to recover



-ii-
and utilize part of the seepage from larger canals and
the fresh groundwater (FGW) occuring at shallow depth
in the northern half of Sanghar. The project also
would expand irrigation in Sanghar and Mirpurkhas by
increasing the capacity of the Nara and Jamrao Canals
and by developing Chotiari Reservoir to regulate addi-
tional water available from the river. The project,
estimated to cost about US$636 million (H), is expected
to be co-financed by the Asian Development Bank (ADB),
the Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA),
the United Kingdom's Overseas Development Administration
(ODA), the Saudi Fund for Development (SF), the Swiss
Development Cooperation (SDC) and the Organization of
Petroleum Exporting Countries Fund for Development
(OPEC Fund). Cost recovery provisions include the
eventual recovery of full operation and maintenance costs
from the beneficiaries.

PAKISTAN
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
LEFT BANK OUTFALL DRAIN STAGE I PROJECT (LBOD-STAGE I)
I. BACKGROUND
Introduction
1.01    The LBOD concept embraces major drainage and agricultural development
and represents the collective efforts of GOP, GOSind, WAPDA, 1UNDP, interna-
tional consultants, and potential cofinanciers, led by the World Bank.
The Stage I Project has evolved from numerous investigations and studies
initiated in the mid-1960s and refined thereafter. The project was subjected
to extensive professional scrutiny and is a milestone in the Bank's
long-standing involvement with Indus Basin development.
1.02    GOP has requested World Bank assistance in financing LBOD-Stage I as
the first phase of the comprehensive LBOD program to provide drainage relief
and improve agricultural production within 11 subareas covering a major
portion of Sind Province. The LBOD catchment area covers the entire left
bank of the Indus River commanded by the Sukkur Barrage and the eastern part
of the irrigation command of the Kotri Barrage. LBOD-Stage I would include
three subareas selected on a drainage-priority basis: Nawabshah, Sanghar and
Nirpurkhas (Nap IBRD 18245). The proposed LBOD-Stage I is designed to ensure
economic viability, even if subsequent stages of the comprehensive LBOD
development program are delayed or not carried out. Provision for LBOD-
Stage I has been made in GOP's Sixth Five-Year Development Plan (FY84-88).
GOP has also indicated that adequate provisions would be made during the
Seventh Plan period (FY89-FY93) to complete the Stage I project by 1993.
1.03    The LBOD concept has been subject to intensive project preparation
during the past 20 years. The Lower Indus Project report, prepared in 1966
by Sir N. MacDonald and Partners Ltd. and Hunting Technical Services Ltd.,
both of the United Kingdom, contains a comprehensive plan for optimum
development of agriculture in the LBOD area. Because of financial and other
constraints, a scaled-down project was prepared by WAPDA in 1972, and
engineering design and construction started in 1974. Since then, construc-
tion (principally of the spinal drain and some branch and smaller drains) has
been ongoing at a relatively slow pace under GOP financing. WAPDA and its
consultant (Sir M. MacDonald Ltd.) updated the LBOD report in 1980 and
produced a Stage I proposal in 1981 as a means of accelerating project
implementation through external financial assistance. However, the updated
proposal did not meet the appraisal standards of external financing agencies.
1.04    The Bank became more directly involved with project preparation in
early 1982. Sir M. MacDonald Ltd. was engaged in late 1982 under UNDP
financing, with the Bank as the Executing Agency, to assist WAPDA to complete



-2-
preparation of LBOD-Stage I. The draft Final Preparation Report was produced
in November 1983 and was jointly reviewed by the Bank and cofinanciers. The
report served as the principal basis for project appraisal.
1.05    During project preparation and appraisal, the Bank additionally
engaged the services of leading consultants in the fields of drainage,
groundwater, irrigation, soil analysis and land classification, operation and
maintenance (O&M), and TW design and construction. OPS staff, particularly
G. Le Moigne, S. Niaz, W. Peters and R. Goodland, and staff of Pakistan
Programs Division, as well as the Resident Mission in Pakistan, contributed
at various stages of project preparation. Staff of ADB, particularly
T. Patterson, closely collaborated with Bank staff. Current members of the
South Asia Projects Department - Irrigation I Division and former divisional
staff J. Kampen and T. Husain also participated. During the past two years
of intensive project preparation, D. Leeuwrik served as project officer and
covered agricultural aspects; R. Anson -was responsible for financial and
economic aspects; S. Baker covered irrigation, engineering, and technical
aspects; and T. H. Yoon provided overall guidance.
Agriculture and Irrigation in Pakistan
1.06    Pakistan's population of 92 million (1984) is growing at an annual
rate of about 3%. Per capita annual income is estimated at US$325 (mid-1984
prices). About 30% of the population have incomes below US$110, the
estimated absolute rural poverty level in Pakistan.
1.07    Agriculture dominates Pakistan's economy.  It contributes about 30%
of Pakistan's gross domestic product (GDP), provides about two-thirds of
total exports, and employs approximately 55X of the country's labor force.
The agriculture sectors growth rates decreased from an annual average of 6%
in the 1960s to 2% in the 1970s, followed by a significant upturn during the
past five years. Major factors in recent improvement have been favorable
weather and the affirmative response of farmers to GOP's policies and
programs for agriculture sector development. Such positive measures include
rationalization of agricultural pricing policies, introduction of programs
for generating quick-yielding benefits, and timely availability of inputs,
such as high-yielding varieties (HYV) of seeds, water, and fertilizers. More
recently, GOP has given emphasis to reducing subsidies, developing an effi-
cient agricultural extension service, upgrading the seed industry, improving
on-farm water management, increasing farm mechanization, rehabilitating
existing canals and drains, and further extending rural electrification, and
it has encouraged private sector participation in the distribution of fer-
tilizers and pesticides.
1.08    Pakistan has a total land area of 79.6 million hectares (N ha).
About one-fourth, 22.1 M ha, is cultivable, of which about 73% (16.2 M ha) is
irrigated. The Indus Irrigation System, commanding 63% (14.0 M ha) of the
total cultivable area, generates about 90% of the nation's total value of
agricultural output. Earliest constructed portions of the Indus Irrigation
System were designed for extensive irrigation, utilizing unregulated river
flows. The first controlled year-round irrigation began in 1859. Irrigation
investments over the past 30 years have aimed at increasing water supplies by
providing reservoir storage and making greater use of groundwater, to
increase both the area irrigated and the cropping intensity. Today, the
Indus Irrigation System comprises three major storage reservoirs (Tarbela,



-3-
Mangla and Chasma), 19 barrages or headvorks, 12 link canals, 43 canal com-
mands and some 89,000 watercourses. Barrages and additional storage, amount-
ing to about 18,000 million cubic meters (H m3), or 15 million acre feet
(maf), of live storage have made possible the provision of significant
amounts of additional irrigation water during critical periods.
Emerzint Drainafe Problems
1.09    The greatly increased continuous use of Indus water for irrigation
(from storage or direct river diversions) has significantly altered the
hydrological balance of the Indus Basin. Seepage losses from irrigation
canals, distributaries, minors, and watercourses and deep percolation from
the irrigated lands have resulted in a gradual rising of the groundwater
table, bringing with it critical problems of waterlogging and salinity over a
vast area. Waterlogging is widespread throughout Punjab and Sind provinces,
where most of the country's food and fiber crops are produced. In the early
1900s, the watertable was generally more than 15 meters (m) below the surface
throughout the Indus Plain. However, by 1978 (when the Indus Basin Salinity
Survey was completed), the watertable in 22% of the Indus Basin was within
1.8 m of the surface, and an additional 30% was within 3 m. The situation
has deteriorated since then. A comprehensive survey conducted in 1961
estimated that about 40,000 ha of land were being lost annually to agricul-
tural production due to waterlogging and/or salinity. Although these
estimates have not been updated, available information suggests that land
abandonment in severely waterlogged areas is continuing.
DrainasRe and Salinity Control in the Indus Basin
1.10    In the early 1960s, to relieve Pakistan's waterlogging and salinity
problems, GOP initiated a series of salinity control and reclamation projects
(SCARP) with public TW installation for drainage and supplemental irrigation
in usable fresh groundwater (FGW) areas. To date, about 12,000 such TWs have
been installed, covering about 35% of Pakistan-s cultivable command area
(CCA). Approximately 90% of these wells are located in Punjab, and most of
the remainder are in Sind. The TW8 have partly alleviated waterlogging and
salinity in these areas, but a large portion of CCA, especially in Sind,
still needs immediate drainage relief. The SCARP experience has demonstrated
that the TWs provide significant drainage relief. Hoeever, operation and
maintenance (&MM) of the TW8 has been deficient due to technical, financial,
and managerial problems. Currently, the principal need is to address the
drainage problem in saline groundwater (SGW) areas and to improve the
efficiency of TWs.
IrriRation in Sind Province
1.11    In Sind, irrigation canals were extended and improved during the late
1800s; by 1910, the irrigated area had reached a peak of 1.4 M ha. Sukkur
Barrage, completed in 1932, increased the annual cultivated area to 2.37 M
ha. The completion of Ghulam Mohammed (now Kotri) Barrage (1955), and Gudu
Barrage (1962) brought the rest of Sind's irrigation system under barrage
control. The total CCA under the three Sind barrages now totals 5.5 M ha.
The additional irrigation water supplied by these barrages has resulted in a
rapid rise of groundwater levels.



-4-
1.12    Sind Province, located in the lover part of the Indus Plain, is the
repository for the plain's drainage waters. Approximately 3.2 M ha, more
than 50X of Sind's total irrigated area, are severely affected by water-
logging and/or salinity problems. Sind's drainage problems are aggravated by
the nearly level terrain and the virtual lack of effective natural surface
drainage due to the construction of roads and irrigation bunds and canals.
Effective drainage and improved water management are urgently needed in
severely affected areas to arrest further land deterioration in the province,
which comprises about 25Z of Pakistan's total irrigated area.
Agriculture Sector Straterv and Bank's Role
1.13    The Bank's role in helping to accelerate Pakistan's agricultural
development includes assisting GOP in (1) fornulating a balanced investment
strategy that would ensure adequate resources for high priority projects to
increase agricultural productivity; (2) improving the effectiveness of
agricultural pricing policies and projects; and (3) mobilizing additional
external resources to help finance priority investment programs. GOP's
investment strategy nas been moving in favor of low-cost investments with
quick-yielding benefits, balanced with larger, strategically selected
infrastructural projects. Bank financing of a number of projects in water
management, irrigation system rehabilitation, salinity control and reclama-
tion, and research and extension and its role as Executing Agency for the
UNDP-financed preparation of major investment projects (such as LBOD) support
this strategy.
1.14    The Revised Action Program Report, prepared by WAPDA with UNDP assis-
tance and the Bank as Executing Agency, confirms the national importance of
the overall LBOD program in developing Pakistan-s water, land, and power
resources. GOP has given priority to LBOD-Stage I in its Sixth Five-Year
Plan (FY84-68). Bank-Group financed projects during its Fifth Five-Year Plan
(FY78-83) included: Reservoir Maintenance (Ln. 2247-PAK); Fourth Drainage
(Cr. 1375-PAR); Irrigation System Reh&ibilitation (Cr. 1239-PAR); Structural
Adjustment Lending (Ln. 2166-PAR and Cr. 1255-PAR); On-Farm Water Management
(Cr. 1163-PAR); Agricultural Research (Cr. 1158-PAR); Baluchistan Minor
Irrigation and Agricultural Development (Cr. 1243-PAR); Grain Storage (Cr.
1157-PAK); Sind Agricultural Extension and Adaptive Research (Cr. 922-PAR);
Punjab Extension and Agricultural Development (Cr. 813-PAK); and Command
Water Management (Cr. 1487-PAK). The Bank Group's lending program in the
water sector during the Sixth Five-Year Plan supports GOP's agriculture
sector strategy with projects including Command Water Management, LBOD-Stage
I, SCARP Transition, Private Tubevells and Rural Electrification, and
follow-up projects to OFWM and Irrigation Systems Rehabilitation.
Rationale for IDA Financin2
1.15    The rationale for Bank involvement in LBOD-Stage I is based on the
Bank's long association with GOP in helping to develop Indus water resources,
the pressing need to address the project areass drainage problems in a timely
and effective manner, and the need to bridge the external financing gap.
Equally important, during the course of numerous preparation missions the
Bank has been able to assist and influence GOP in designing and implementing
a technically and economically viable project, as proposed, as well as to
strengthen its sectoral policy dialogue with GOP and fulfill its coordinating
role with potential cofinanciers.



-5-
II. THE PROJECT AREA
General
2.01    The LBOD-Stage I project area would include Nawabshah, Sanghar, and
Mirpurkhas (Tharparkar), serving a CCA of about 516,000 ha within a gross
command area (GC&) of 578,000 ha (Map IBRD 18245).
Climate
2.02    Irrigation is essential for sustained agriculture in the project
area. The climate is hot and arid, with average maximum temperatures
approaching 43 degrees C in May-June. The coolest months are November-
FebrQary, when temperatures average 18-27 degrees C. Frosts are rare south
of Navabshah. Rainfall is low and erratic and occurs principally during
July-August. Average annual rainfall decreases from about 175 millimeters
(mm) in Mirpurkhas to 125 mm in Nawabshah. Significant rainfall is often
concentrated in one or two annual storms which may cause extensive flooding.
Evaporation rates, totalling 2,250-2,375 mm per year, vary from 11 ummday in
the hottest months to only 3 mm/day in winter.
Topography and Soils
2.03    The terrain in the project area is generally flat.  Some relief is
provided by channel remnants and bar deposits from old river courses and
their tributaries. Average slope is only 0.014U southeastwards, away from
the present course of the Indus River. Natural drainage is ill-defined and,
to a large extent, blocked by roads and irrigation bunds and channels. The
Dhoro Puran provides some drainage relief for Mirpurkhas, and the Nara Valley
system provides surface drainage for about 300 square kilometers (sq km)
north of Sanghar.
2.04    Soils are of recent alluvial origin and, when adequately drained and
managed, are generally suitable for irrigated agriculture. Textures are
closely related to depositional conditions, and although stratification is
complex, the majority of soils range from fine sandy loam to silty clay, with
silty loams and silty clay loams being most common. True clays and sands are
rare, at least in the upper alluvium. All soils contain calcium carbonate
and most contain traces of gypsum. Salinity is widespread. The severity and
distribution of soil salinity, as determined from average electrical conduc-
tivity measurements made by WAPDA, are listed in Table 2.1:



-6-
Table 2.1: ESTIMATED PERCENTAGE OF CCA IN EACH SUBAREA BY
SOIL SALINITY LEVEL

Extremely Saline            >32.0                2           0           0
Non-cultivated land is generally highly saline and requires leaching prior to
cropping.  Salinity has been increasing since the first lover Indus Plan data
were collected in 1963-64, showing the concentrating effects of irrigation
without drainage.
2.05    Chemical characterization shows a high sodium (alkali) concentration
in highly saline soils, but the total area affected is relatively small
(12Z). Non-saline sodic soils occupy less than 2% of the area. With ade-
quate water and drainage to allow leaching of excess salts, lands with ade-
quate infiltration and internal permeabilities should respond well to
reclamation.
2.06    According to a land classification using United States Bureau of
Reclamation criteria adapted to LBOD catchment conditions (Annex 2), 80%
(492,880 ha) of the total area is suitable for irrigation, if necessary
drainage is provided (Class 1, 2 or 3 land). Hydraulic conductivities are
adequate for t.ubewell drainage throughout Nawabshah and Sanghar and most of
Nirpurkhas. About 51,230 ha, chiefly in Mirpurkhas, have both soil and
drainage deficiencies usually associated with fine-textured substrata. Lands
requiring tile drainage comprise only about 9% of the total project area.
About 20% of the land, being mostly out of command or prone to flooding, is
not suitable for drainage (Annex 2).
Irrigation Water Supplies
2.07    Canal System.  The three subareas are located in the perennial com-
mand of Sukkur barrage. Nawabshah is served by the Rohri Canal, and Sanghar
and Mirpurkhas by the Eastern Nara Canal. The first stage of the Rohri Canal
remodelling is currently underway with ADB financing. The maximum discharge
in the Nara Canal is still within the 1932 design limits, but bed erosion
below the head regulator is a major problem. The eroded material deposited
downstream has raised the bed level of the Jamrao Canal by about 3 meters
(m), necessitating the raising of banks and modifying regulators and bridges.
There is an urgent need to control upstream erosion and prevent sediment from
entering the Jamrao System. Since completion of Tarbela Reservoir in 1976,
the Rohri and Nara Canal systems have run at full capacity during kharif
(summer _rop season) and at near-capacity during rabi (winter crop season).
Unless their capacities are increased, scope for increasing water supplies to
the project area is nil during kharif and is limited to about lOZ during
rabi.



-7-
2.08    Groundwater.  The project area generally is underlain by an extensive
alluvial aquifer with a predominantly sandy upper layer, 60-90 m thick, which
is hydraulically connected with the watertable. This layer is thinner and
less distinct in the eastern part of Mirpurkhas. There are no hydraulic
constraints to the use of TWs for irrigation or drainage in most of the
project area, but aquifer conditions are less favorable in Mirpurkhas.
2.09    There has been a dramatic rise in the watertable in the project area
during the past 20 years. Currently, about 30% of the area has a watertable
at a depth of less than 1.2 m, which has induced salinization of uncropped
land. Since most of the groundwater is saline, possibilities for using it
for irrigation are limited. In some areas within Nawabshah and Sanghar, SGW
is overlain by a layer of FGW, generally less than 76 m in overall thickness.
This FGW cannot be recovered efficiently by conventional TWs, but may be
recoverable using scavenger wells 1/ which simultaneously pump from the SGW
and the FGW aquifer. The main constraint to watertable control by the
removal of groundwater is the lack of a disposal system for the saline dis-
charge.
Electrification
2.10    Power is supplied to the project area by a dual circuit 132 kV trans-
mission line from WAPDA's national grid system. About 57% of the power
supplied is from hydroelectric sources in northern Pakistan, and the balance
from thermal plants at Gudu, Sukkur, and Hyderabad. The project area is
served by seven grid stations with an installed capacity of about 175
megawatts (MW). All towns and a substantial number of villages have
electrical power.
Population and Land Tenure
2.11    The population in the project area is about 1.2 million, of which
73% are classified as rural (1981 Census). Approximately 49Z of the rural
population is in Nawabshah, 24% in Sanghar, and 27Z in Mirpurkhas. The
project area includes 7.6% of the rural population of Sind. There are an
estimated 113,000 farm holdings. The rural population growth rate in the
project area has averaged 3.5Z annually since 1961, compared with a national
population growth rate of 3% during this period.
2.12    The majority of farm land is ireld by owners with more than 20 ha
(1972 Agricultural Census). There appears to have been little change since
1972, despite land reforms in 1972 and 1977, which reduced the maximum per-
missible holding to approximately 40 ha of irrigated land. The average
operational farm unit is less than half the size of the average land holding.
About 70% of the total number of operational farm units are less than 5 ha,
and about 90% are less than 10 ha. Approximately 60% of the units are farmed
by share-cropping tenants (haris) and 30% by landowners; the balance is
j1 Scavenger TWs are specially designed wells employing two pumps at
different levels, to allow safe extraction of FGW from a layer overlying
SGW.



-8-
leased or farmed on a contract basis. No significant change in land owner-
ship or tenure pattern is expected in the near future. Table 2.2 shows the
breakdown of land ownership and operational units by farm size:

Land Use
2.13    Approximately 25% of the CCA is abandoned or has never been cul-
tivated. Cropping intensity is low, generally less than 50% in any season,
mainly due to a shortage of irrigation water. Due to rising watertables and
salinization of land, agricultural production gradually is being concentrated
on a smaller area, resulting in an increase of abandoned land, a decrease in
fallow area, and a greater amount of double cropping.
2.14    Annual cropping intensities vary considerably within and among canal
commands: Nawabshah 70-140%; Sanghar 30-135% (mostly in the range of
60-115%); and Mirpurkhas 60-90%, although somewhat higher intensities are
obtained in northern fruit growing areas.
Crops and Yields
2.15    The principal crops, cotton and wheat, account for at least
two-thirds of the cropped area during kharif and rabi, respectively. Sugar-
cane is an important crop in Nawabshah and Mirpurkhas, and oilseeds (mainly
grown in rabi) are widely grown in Nawabshah and Sanghar. Fodder also is
grown, particularly in Sanghar and Mirpurkhas, in both seasons. Mangoes are
an important perennial crop, particularly in Mirpurkhas. The area devoted to
rice and pulses is very small. During the past 20 years, the areas cul-
tivated with cotton and wheat have risen relative to other crops. This trend
reflects the relative profitability of both crops and their suitability for
double cropping.
2.16    The present overall cropping intensity of about 81Z in the project
area is achieved by spreading water during kharif and relying partly on soil
moisture storage and capillary rise from the watertable to meet peak water
requirements. Leaching of salts is deferred to the end of the season or to
the pre-irrigation of the next crop. This practice maximizes the returns to
kharif water supplies, but results in lower efficiency of water use in rabi,
which has important implications for drainage requirements. Fertilizer use
is universal for cotton, wheat, and sugarcane. Between 150 kg and 230 kg of



-9-
nutrient are applied per ha. Fertilizer application on cotton is near the
maximum economic use for present varieties and prices, but poor pest control
is still a major constraint to higher yields. While most farmers use insec-
ticides, they do not apply them well enough to obtain substantial yield
benefits.
2.17    Lack of good seed is a problem affecting all crops, especially
cotton. High-yielding varieties have been introduced recently for wheat and
sugarcane, but generally the seed has not been renewed. Since 1971/72,
average wheat yields have risen 50%, while cotton and sugarcane yields have
increased only 16%. Oilseed yields have not changed. All crop yields are
still relatively low, partly because of the effects of rising watertables.
Cotton and wheat yields decline when watertable depth is less than 1.8 m and
1.5 m, respectively. Also, salinization of uncropped areas has led to
increased double cropping. With double cropping, the time available for land
levelling and preparation is generally inadequate, causing a further reduc-
tion in crop yields.
Agricultural Inputs and Services
2.18    Fertilizer, Pesticides, and Seed.  In Sind, fertilizer is distributed
by three local manufacturers, or by the Sind Agricultural Supplies Organiza-
tion if imported through the federal Department of Fertilizer Import. Fer-
tilizer subsidies are being phased out. Supplies are generally adequate, but
imports sometimes arrive late. In 1980, pesticide distribution was trans-
ferred from the extension service to the private sector, and the subsidy on
pesticides was abolished. Farmers rely mainly on their own stocks for wheat
seed, and on local ginneries and markets for cotton seed. The Sind Seed
Corporation is responsible for seed production, processing, and certification
while the Sind Agricultural Supplies Organization is responsible for dis-
tribution. Relative to requirements, production of improved certified seed
is low. There are serious technical problems at the main Sind Seed Corpora-
tion processing plant in Sakrand, and the Corporation and Organization have
not fully coordinated their activities. Some improvements were made under
the Bank-supported Seed Industry Development Project (Cr. 620-PAK), now
closed. Continued GOSind efforts to strengthen the production, processing,
and distribution of seed to meet increased demand are required; based on
on-going and proposed plans, such improvements are expected to materialize
during the project's implementation period.
2.19    Azricultural Credit.  Institutional credit for agriculture is
provided by the Agricultural Development Bank of Pakistan (ADBP), the
cooperative banks, and commercial banks. Commercial banks and the ADBP have
branches within the project area, but cooperative credit has almost ceased
because of farmers' outstanding debts. The commercial banks concentrate on
seasonal loans. Landowners normally supply the inputs required by their
tenants on an interest-free basis and, therefore, a direct link between
tenant farmers and credit institutions is not a critical concern to the
project. Both ADBP and commercial banks have been expanding credit distribu-
tion in the project area, and further expansion of ADBP credit facilities is
expected to continue under the Fifth ADBP project supported by the World
Bank, Asian Development Bank, and the International Fund for Agricultural
Development. Agricultural credit availability is unlikely to be a major
constraint on future agricultural development in the project area.



-10-
2.20    Agricultural Research and Extension.  Six researcb institutes and
the agricultural university at Tando Jam conduct agricultural research in
Sind. The overall output of these institutions with respect to solving the
practical needs of Sind's farming community has been relatively low.
Field-oriented adaptive research, being expanded under the IDA-supported
Adaptive Research and Extension Project, should ensure the minimum level of
local research required to support agricultural development. Agricultural
extension is currently being strengthened in five districts in Sind.
Mirpurkhas, the only subarea not currently covered, is expected to be
included in an expanded agricultural extension project, for which allocation
has been made in the Sixth Five-Year plan. Therefore, extension activities
in the LBOD-Stage I area are expected to be adequate.
2.21    On-Farm Water Manaxement.  The Directorate of OFWM of GOSind's
Department of Agriculture has 15 field teams operating in the Rohri command
area under the IDA-supported OFWM project. Services provided to farmers
include design, survey and supervision of watercourse improvements, sub-
sidized hire of equipment, and contribution towards the cost of materials.
The OFWM project is operative in Navabshah, where it would be strengthened,
and it would be extended to Sanghar and Mirpurkhas.
2.22    Farm Power.  Bullocks are the main source of farm pover, with hired
tractors being used increasingly. The Directorate of Agricultural Engineer-
ing maintains a fleet of bulldozers, available for hire to farmers for coarse
land-levelling (± 5 cm). It is also preparing to provide a general tractor-
hire service on a pilot scale. Most farm labor is supplied by haris and
their families and, to a lesser extent, by small owner-cultivators and
seasonal hired labor. Adequate labor is available for cultivation, and
tractor and bullock power are considered sufficient for future agricultural
development in the project area.
III. THE PROJECT
Obiectives and Scone
3.01    The project, to be implemented over an eight-year period (FY86-FY93),
would consist of a major outfall drain and a comprehensive drainage system
to: (a) provide surface drainage for about 516,000 ha of CCA; (b) provide
subsurface drainage relief for about 392,000 ha of CCA; and (c) transport
excess water and salt out of the area, thus reversing deterioration of the
land resource base due to waterlogging and salinity. The project would
include improvements in irrigation and OFWM and institutional improvements to
ensure adequate operation and maintenance (O&M). Existing water-use
efficiencies would be improved and unused surface flows would be stored for
later use in the project area to increase cropping intensities from the
present 81X to about 117Z, including reclamntion of some abandoned land.



-ll-
Project Description
3.02    The LBOD-Stage I project would include the following vorks:
(i)  Spinal Drain System
(a) Completion of main outfall drain;
(b) Construction of a bifurcation weir at the junction of the
Kadhan Pateji Outfall Drain (KPOD) and the Dhoro Puran
Outfall Drain (DPOD) to limit the flow in DPOD to 57 cumecs
(2,000 cusecs) and exclude all ultra-saline effluent;
(c) Remodelling of DPOD to a capacity of 57 cumecs (2,000 cusecs);
(d) Remodelling of KPOD to a capacity of 85 cumecs (3,000 cusecs)
to carry drainage flows from the spinal drain and the
existing Kotri drain;
(e) Construction of an 85 cumec-capacity (3,000 cusecs) tidal
link from Pateji Dhand to Shah Samando tidal creek; and
(f) Provision of maintenance equipment for both the upper and
lower reaches of LBOD-Stage I, and the construction of
vorkshops and depots at Khoski and Nirpurkhas.
(ii)  Drainage Component
(a) Construction of a surface drainage network covering about
578,000 ha of GCA;
(b)  Installation of about 1,400 drainage TVs of a capacity of
14-57 liters per second (I/s) (.5-2.0 cusecs) for subsurface
drainage of about 286,000 ha of CCA;
Xc) Subject to site-specific experimentation, installation of
about 850 scavenger TWs of about 42 1/s-capacity (1.5 cusec)
to provide subsurface drainage of about 84,000 ha of CCA.
About 70% of the discharge would be FGW, which would be
introduced into the irrigation system to supplement existing
supplies;
(d) Installation of interceptor drains, designed to recover about
30% of present seepage losses from 480 km of major canals,
and provision of about 160 pumping stations to lift the
recovered fresh water to smaller canals and watercourses
offtaking from the main canals;
(e) Installation of a tile drainage system and associated pumping
stations for subsurface drainage of about 22,000 ha of CCA
in the Mirpurkhas area;



-12-
(f)  Construction of about 4,400 km of 11 kV distribution line and
enlargement of four grid stations (132/11 kV and 66/11 kV)
to provide power for drainage TVs, scavenger TVs, and tile
and interceptor drain pumping stations; and
(g)  Provision of maintenance equipment for surface drains, TWs,
pumps, tile and interceptor drains, and construction of work-
shops and depots at Nawabshah, Sanghar, and Mirpurkhas.
(iii) Irrigation Works
(a)  Remodelling of Nara Canal to increase the effective capacity
from 365 cumecs (13,000 cusecs) to about 560 cumecL (20,000
cusecs);
(b)  Rehabilitation of the Jamrao Canal headworks, construction of
silt extractors, and remodelling of the Jaurao Canal network;
(c)  Construction of Chotiari Reservoir to provide essential storage
of about 864 M m3 (700,000 acre feet), thus allowing regulation
and use of part of the excess flow available in the Indus River;
and
(d)  Improvement of about 920 watercourses to standards established
under ongoing OFWl programs, including precision land level-
ling of about 26,000 ha of CCA.
(iv) Consulting Services and Trainin&

up to 264 man-months of local consultants for assistance in

Cb) Provision of appropriate in-service and mid-career training
programs for professional staff of agencies concerned with
project implementation and subsequent O&M.

main physical (non-crop) parameters; and
(b) Evaluation study to assess the project's impact.
Detailed Proiect Components
3.03    Spinal Drain (base cost US$8.6 M). Drain excavation and related
structures to complete remaining sections of about 250 km of spinal drain
would be completed within two years of project implenentation (using GOP
funds), thereby providing an unimpeded outfall channel of adequate dimension
to carry storm runoff and drainage effluent from Nawabshah, Saughar, and
Mirpurkhas to DPOD and KPOD. The spinal drain would have an approximate
capacity of 57 cumecs (2,000 cusecs) in Nawabshah, increasing to 71 cumecs
(2,500 cusecs) and 85 cumecs (3,000 cusecs) in Sanghar and Mirpurkhas,



-13-
respectively, and to 113 cumecs (4,000 cusecs) at the bifurcation structure
leading to KPOD and DPOD. Design capacities have been based on estimated TW
discharge from the three subareas and the maximum canal escape in the system.
Stormwater would be included in the channel freeboard allowance (1.0 m) and
all inflow to the system would be controlled. Channel design for reaches
carrying discharges exceeding 28 cumecs would incorporate a deeper central
section to reduce meandering. Maximum channel depth would be limited to 4.3
m for ease of construction and maintenance. Design of the longitudinal
section would minimize earthwork costs and take into account desired gradient
to convey design storms. Design and construction of structures for the
spinal drain and the KPOD, DPOD, and tidal link drains would take into
account wrOibilities of and requirements for future expansion of the
long-term comprehensive LBOD project. The timely completion of the spinal
drain would be of significant importance to the project as a whole, since the
phasing of all other components is dependent on this. An assurance was
obtained from GOP that adequate provisions would be made to complete the
spinal drain and associated structures up to Nawabshah to required capacity
by July 31, 1986.
3.04    KPOD and DPOD (base cost US$18.7 M).  The spinal drain would outfall
through the remodelled KPOD and tidal link to Shah Samando Creek and into the
Arabian Sea (Map IBRD 18245). KPOD would be designed to carry a flow of up
to 62 cumecs from the spinal drain and approximately 28 cumecs from the Kotri
drain. Construction work would include widening the existing KPOD channel,
constructing the bifurcation headworks on the Dhoro link for automatic
separation of the saline baseflow and high stormwater flows, and replacing
6 crossings with 5 new structures, mainly due to realignment. Six existing
structures would be utilized without enlargement, including the Khadhan-Ali
Bundar road bridge and the aqueduct for the Fuleli escape. The channP1 bed
would be maintained at constant slope from the junction with the spinal drain
to the head of the tidal link. Inspection road bridges across 8 existing
branch drains would be replaced and 56 new surface inlets constructed. The
bifurcation structure would prevent saline baseflow in the spinal drain from
entering the DPOD system. Flow into DPOD would begin only when discharge in
the spinal drain exceeds 42 cumecs (1,500 cusecs); it would peak in DPOD at
57 cumecs (2,000 cusecs) when the flow in the spinal drain reaches its maxi-
mum of 113 cumecs (4,000 cusecs). DPOD principally would provide an escape
for stormwater flows to the Shakoor Dhand, as at present. The Dhoro Link and
DPOD would be enlarged to a capacity of about 57 cumecs, partly by excavation
downstream and by constructing bunds upstream, to convey high stormwater
flows without submerging the Dhoro Link headworks. Maintenance roads would
be provided on both sides of KPOD and DPOD.
3.05    Tidal Link (base cost US$21.3 M).  A 38-km link channel would be
constructed from the Pateji Dhand (at the downstream end of KPOD) to the Shah
Samando tidal creek. The channel would operate independently of the Pateji
Dhand and the Rann of Kutch, and the channel berm would isolate the channel
from high water levels in the Rann of Kutch. The channel would have a bed
width of 40 m and, at its outfall to the creek, the width would be gradually
increased to 114 m to avoid critical velocities during periods of low tide.
Channel design would provide the most efficient hydraulic gradient for the
outfall. Under design conditions, intrusion of sea water would occur up to
19 km inland; however, such intrusion would be of short duration and the
drain would operate mostly at outfall levels below water levels in the Rann
of Kutch. Technical details of the spinal drain, KPOD and DPOD, and tidal



-14-
link are available in the project file and cost estimates are presented in
Annex 3, Table 3a and b.
3.06    Drainage Work in Subareas.  Groundwater levels in Nawabshah, Sanghar,
and Mirpurkhas subareas would be controlled to safe cropping levels by
installing drainage TWs, scavenger TWs, interceptor and subsurface drains,
and surface drains, as discussed in paras 3.07-3.11. The works provided in
the three subareas would be designed to remove the additional groundwater
inflow anticipated from the increased irrigation water resulting from the
Rohri and Nara/Jamrao Canal remodelling. Allowance would also be made for
water losses from the disposal channels and the surface drainage system. The
drainage TWs would be designed to operate about 60% of the time, on average,
and to maintain the watertable at a depth of about 1.5-2.0 m. The tile
drainage design would maintain an average watertable depth of about 1.5 m in
the tile drainage area. The capacity of the subsurface and surface drainage
network (size of drains, and the number, size, and spacing of TWs and tile
drains) would be determined by the quantity of drainable effluent in the
LBOD-Stage I catchment area expected from subsurface drainage, stormwater
drainage, and canal escapes.
3.07    Drainag:e Tubewells (base cost US$35.6 M). Because of the excellent
aquifer conditions prevailing throughout much of Sind, most of the 1,400
drainage TWs installed in the three subareas would be relatively
high-capacity, conventional drainage TWs. However, some lower-capacity TWs
of special design would be required for the unusually fine aquifer sands
found mainly in Mirpurkhas. In Nawabshah, about 245 TWs of 57 l/s-capacity
would be constructed and spaced, on average, about 2,300 m apart. Some 140
TWs of either 42 l/s- or 57 1/s-capacity would be provided along major canals
to remove seepage losses not picked up by the interceptor drains. Seepage
losses in individual reaches of the main and branch canals, rather than
average drainable surplus values, would be used for detailed design of the
interceptor drains and TWs. In Sanghar, most of the 230 regular drainage TWS
of 57 1/s-capacity would be provided at approximate spacing of 1,700-2,680
m. The remaining TWs (about 70) would be located along major canals to
control seepage flows to the watertable. In Mirpurkhas, about 129 TWs of 42
1/s-capacity would be provided. The high-capacity TWs in the subareas would
be completed with 200 mm-diameter screens. Consideration would be given to
using fiberglass for the screens and pump housing to reduce weight and corro-
sion potential. Each TW would discharge into an open disposal channel or
pipe (about 5%) linking the well with the nearest drain. The special design
TWs in Mirpurkhas (586 of 715 TWa required) would have a relatively low
discharge (14 1/s or 21 l/s) and would use stainless steel screens with a
very small slot width designed on the basis of sediment sizes. Three
different methods of screening fine sediments would be evaluated on a
pilot-basis (10 wells each) before finalizing the design of the bulk of these
TWs: (a) continuous-slot wire wrapped screens; (b) two-stage gravel packs
with standard fiberglass screen; and (c) synthetic molded-on or wrap-around
filter. About half of these wells would pump directly into pipe disposal
lines (in lieu of open surface drains) to limit loss of saline effluent in
agricultural areas and avoid disruption to the irrigation system. The length
of disposal channel/pipe from low discharge wells would average 1200 m. All
TWs would have appropriately designed gravel filter-graded envelopes (gravel
packs) and would be provided with permanent access roads. The TWa would be
equipped with integrated pump-motor sets consisting of vertically mounted
electric motors and in-line shaft, deep-well turbine pumps, which have proven



-15-
satisfactory in other completed SCARP projects. To combat the corrosive
effect of the highly saline groundwater to be pumped, corrosion-resis.ant and
chemically inert materials would be used in TW components. Remote con-
trol/monitoring of TW pump operation was considered at appraisal and may be
adopted eventually under the project. Costs of converting to a fully
automated system would be relatively small and would be about equal to the
additional cost of manual operation of such TWs during project implementa-
tion, which is based on one operator for two TWs.
3.08    Scavenger Tubewelle (base cost US$21.1).  About 5,000 ha of CCA in
Navabshah and 79,400 ha in Sanghar would be drained by scavenger TWs designed
to recover FGW from the layer of good quality water overlying the SGW. A
total of about 850 scavenger TWs would be installed (about 92 in Nawabshah
and 758 in Sanghar) following a design similar to the 42 1/s-capacity
drainage TWs, except that a larger-diameter (250 mm) screen would be provided
to enable the use of a lower pump to withdraw the underlying SGW and maintain
hydraulic FGW and SGW equilibrium. About two-thirds (28 1/) of the total
output of each well would be PGW discharged by the upper pump into a canal or
watercourse for irrigation use. The flow from the lower pump (SGW) would be
discharged into the drainage disposal channel/pipe. While most of the
scavenger TWs would be 42 1/s-capacity, in places where the FGW/SGW interface
is deeper a limited number of TWs would have a capacity of as much as 85 1/s.
As scavenger TW design evolves, pilot well installations would be tested
early in the project to finalize the design concept. Depending on the per-
formance of the pilot scavenger TWs, conventional drainage TWS could replace
them to accomplish the necessary drainage without jeopardizing project
viability.
3.09    Tile Drainage (base cost US$21.6M).  About 1,875 km of tile drains
would be installed in the 22,000 ha of CCA in Mirpurkhas where aquifer condi-
tions are unsuitable for TW drainage. PVC corrugated pipe laterals would be
installed with gravel envelope at average depths of 1.8-2.4 m. They would be
connected to PVC corrugated pipe collectors laid with gravel envelope at
2.5-3.5 m depth. Manholes would be provided as needed. A total of 52 pump-
ing stations, located near main drains to avoid construction of special
disposal channels and equipped with 2-3 electric centrifugal pumps of 14 l/s-
or 28 l/s-capacity, would lift drainage flows from the collector sumps to the
surface drains.
3.10    Interceptor Drains (base cost US$21.4).  Approximately 580 km of
horizontal interceptor drains (subsurface tile drains) would be installed
along main and branch canals to intercept a significant part of the seepage
from these canals, to reduce drainage requirements, and to provide supplemen-
tal water for irrigation. Generally, the drains would be installed about
30-50 m from the canal centerline on either one or both sides of main and
branch canals experiencing major seepage losses, and they would recover an
estimated 30% of such seepage. Each drain would comprise a single line of
PVC corrugated perforated pipe with gravel envelope installed at 2.7-3.7 m
depth. Pipe diameter would vary from 200 mm to 300 mm, depending on soil
conditions, seepage rate, and drain length. The maximum length of each
collector drain would be about 2000 m, and two such drains would discharge to
a single pump equipped with two 28 l/s- or 56 1/s-centrifugal pumps. Dis-
charge from the interceptor drains would be pumped directly into a water-
course or returned to the canal. Installation of the interceptors would be
coordinated with canal remodelling and improvement of watercourses.



-16-
3.11    Surface Drainage (base cost US$28.4 N). A network of main, branch,
and sub-drains would be constructed to dispose of saline drainage effluent
from TVs and tile drains and the runoff from storms and excess irrigation.
The surface drainage system would be unlined earthen channels with structures
to control the flow of water in the drains. It would include 9 main and
branch drains in the three subareas. To the extent possible, drain alignment
would be fixed by command boundaries and would be adjusted to utilize areas
of abandoned land or existing natural drainage ways. Drainage system
capacity would be based primarily on discharge requirements of drainage wells
and tile drains, with additional discharge capacity for canal escapes. About
0.3 m of freeboard would be allowed for sub-drains and 0.6 a of freeboard for
branch and main drains to accommodate stormwater drainage, when the drainage
TWs would be turned off. A 1.0 m freeboard would be provided in the spinal
drain. Drain inlets (one per watercourse for Sanghar and Mirpurkhas and two
per watercourse for Nawabshah) would be constructed for the direct disposal
of this water to the spinal drain. About 1,600 drain inlets (Nawabshah-440,
Sanghar-570, and Mirpurkhas-590) would be constructed. Maximum surface water
discharge into the drains would be about 22 cumecs in Nawabshah, 28 cumecs in
Sanghar, and 33 cumecs in Mirpurkhas. The surface drainage system would
substantially reduce the time required, in days, to evacuate runoff from a
100 mm-storm (occurring once in about 5 years) from 13 to 3-1/2 in Nawabshah,
from 17 to less than 3-1/2 in Sanghar, and from 14-1/2 to 3 in Mirpurkhas.
Existing surface drains providing drainage for about 31,200 ha in Saughar,
which discharge to a pump station on the Mithrao Canal, would be enlarged and
connected to the spinal drain. Natural drainage provided by the Dhoro Puran
for about 30,000 ha in Hirpurkhas would be replaced by a more efficient
drainage system for both surface and subsurface drainage flows.
3.12    On-Farm Water Management (base cost US$18.6 M). Under the project
about half of the watercourses in the project area would be improved (270 in
Nawabshah, 310 in Sanghar, and 340 in Mirpurkhas). Watercourse improvement
would follow the same development criteria followed in the OFWM project
(Cr. 1163-PAK), and would incorporate any changes introduced in the second
OFWM project (currently under preparation). Civil works, including
earthwork, farm drains, permanent structures, lining (30% in saline
groundwater areas), and precision land levelling (about 10% of the area under
improved watercourses) would be carried out in accordance with GOP's OFWM
National Standards for Practices and Materials, Ministry of Food, Agriculture
and Cooperatives, June 1980, or subsequent issues thereof acceptable to IDA.
The present district-level OFWM unit in Nawabshah would be strengthened, and
new units would be set up in Sanghar and Mirpurkhas. Under the project, 14
new OFWM field teams would be formed to provide technical assistance to
farmers, who would provide the unskilled labor required. OFWM teams would
include drainage specialists to advise farmers on farm drainage. Vehicles,
equipment, and technical assistance also would be provided. Watercourse
renovation work would be closely coordinated with other project activities,
such as drainage and canal remodelling work.
3.13    Electrification (base cost US$51.4 M).  Since the existing rural
11 kV distribution system is heavily loaded and no allowance for project
works was made in the extension already planned, a separate electrification
system to provide power for the TWs and drainage pumps would be constructed.
It would consist of 1,100 km of 11 kV overhead transmission lines in
Nawabshah, 1,950 km in Sanghar, and 1,350 km in Mirpurkhas. A pole-mounted



-17-
transformer protected by dropout fuses would be provided at each well or pump
station. Power to the 11 kV lines would be supplied from existing grid
stations, with new transformer bays provided at four of these stations
(Nawabshah, Sanghar, Mirpurkhas, and Kandiari). Existing and planned power
generation capacity is judged adequate to absorb the project's requirements
(Annex 1, Chart 5) but, inasmuch as the power requirements for the project
would be a substantial part of the overall power available in the Province
and the drainage system is dependent on the timely and efficient operation of
the drainage wells and pumping stations, it is important that WAPDA provides
the full energy and capacity requirements of the project on a timely basis.
3.14    Irrigation Works.  Adequate subsurface drainage would permit effec-
tive utilization of additional irrigation water for increased crop produc-
tion. Under LBOD-Stage I, supplemental water would be provided within the
CCA to about 101,250 ha in Sanghar and 77,000 ha in Mirpurkhas. Additional
water also is being provided for the Nawabshah area under the ongoing Rohri
Canal remodelling work being financed by ADB. An assurance was obtained from
GOP and GOSind that GOSind would ensure that the Rohri Canal Remodelling
progresses at a rate such that the Rohri Canal command area falling within
the project area receives adequate irrigation not later than January 1, 1990,
and that adequate funds would be provided for completing such works.
Theoretical water requirements in the project area and expected overall water
supplies or deficits under with project conditions are shown in Annex 1,
Table 1.
3.15    Nara Canal Remodelling (base cost US$23.5 M).  The current capacity
of the Nara Canal is about 364 cumecs. Under the project, the upper reach
(175 km) of the canal to the Jamrao head would be enlarged to a maximum
capacity of 560 cumecs, thus providing about 196 cumecs of capacity for
diversion to Chotiari Reservoir of water currently escaping to the Arabian
Sea during July and August. Bed erosion in the upper reach of the steeply
sloping Nara Canal that currently threatens the head regulator would be
controlled, and siltation of the reach above the Jamrao head would be reduced
by constructing fall (drop) structures to reduce the hydraulic gradient.
Also, some canal bank remodelling would be carried out in the lower reaches
to increase capacity. About half of the water developed would be utilized by
LBOD-Stage I through the remodelled Jamrao Canal system. The remainder would
be utilized in areas outside the Stage I area, including those to be covered
by the proposed Makhi-Faresh project.
3.16    Jamrao Canal Remodellin2 (base cost US$28.6 M).  A canal parallel to
the existing one, with its own head regulator, would be constructed at a
lower level to convey about 55% of the total Jamrao Canal discharge. The
existing canal would continue to serve high-level offtakes in the Sanghar
area, while the new canal would act mainly as a feeder for the system down-
stream. An additional 60 km of parallel canal below the existing West
Branch, not to be remodelled, also would be provided. A total of 345 km of
branch, distributary, and minor canals would be enlarged or duplicated, and
all existing structures would be either remodelled or replaced. To reduce
the sediment load entering the Jamrao system, 6 vortex-tube silt extractors
would be installed to trap 80% of the bed material load, which would be
discharged into a settling basin kept clear by dredger.
3.17    Chotiari Reservoir (base cost US$21.0 M).  The Chotiari Reservoir
would be formed by enclosing the existing Chotiari Lakes with 43 km of



-18-
embanknent, 3ocated mainly along the Nara Canal, increasing live storage to
about 864 XmA (700,000 acre feet).  The upper water level would be 26.5 n
relative level (RL). The present water level is about 21 m RL. Hydraulic
constraints on the outlet to the proposed Makhi-Faresh link, limit the dead
storage level to about 21 m RL. This outlet would discharge up to 64 cumecs
to the new link to supplement direct canal supplies. Diversions to the
reservoir would be made at the Jam-ao headworks through the Ranto Escape by a
new 19 km-long feeder channel capable of discharging about 200 cumecs (7,000
cusecs). The reservoir surface area would vary seasonally from about
6,900-22,700 ha and evaporation losses would be high. The cost of the addi-
tional storage is partly justified by the Makhi-Faresh project. Assurances
were obtained from GOP and GOSind that the water supplies made available by
the Nara and Jamrao Canal remodelling work and the construction of Chotiari
Reservoir would be fully utilized in LBOD-Stage I and adjoining areas,
including the proposed Makhi-Faresh Project.
3.18    Maintenance Equipment. Depots, and Workshops (base Cost US$25.2 M).
Maintenance depots would be constructed at Nawabshah, Sanghar, and
Mirpurkhas. Equipment for maintenance would include long-reach excavators,
graders and support vehicles for drain and access-road maintenance, and a
truck-mounted crane for handling transformers, pumps, and motors. The depots
would have workshopu for routine maintenance of the equipment and vehicles,
and mobile workshop trucks would carry out minor repairs and servicing of
machinery in the field. Adequate staff, transport, and equipment would be
provided for the repair of structures. A workshop would be constructed at
Khoski for maintenance of the lower outfall drain. Equipment, including
dredgers, long-reach draglines, hydraulic excavators, graders, and various
support vehicles, would be provided for maintenance of the outfall system.
Two small depots would be constructed to service dredgers for maintenance of
DPOD and main outfall.
3.19    Technical Assistance and Training (base cost US$9.3 M).  The project
would provide a total of 878 man-months of internationally selected consult-
ing services to assist project management in overall planning and monitoring
of project implementation; special studies; preparation of design criteria,
final designs, specifications, tender documents and contracts, evaluation of
bids; supervision of construction; and preparation of operation and main-
tenance procedures. The consulting firm would be associated with a suitable
local firm and would include drainage design engineers, groundwater
engineer/hydrogeologists, irrigation design engineers, construction
engineers, drainage construc;ion specialists, an irrigation construction
specialist, an electrical engineer, a hydraulics engineer, and an ecologist.
In addition, provision has been made for local supervisory consultants (264
man-months) to assist in the implementation of the OFWM program (Annex 6).
3.20    The project would provide in-service, local and overseas training of
professional staff. About 30 engineers and other professional staff from
WAPDA and GOSind would receive mid-career and in-service training in O&M
management to facilitate efficient implementation and proper OEM of the
completed drainage system. The training would include the use of
micro-computers/processors for improved control and monitoring of the project
works. Visiting experts, coordinated by the overall project consultants,
would conduct nUmerous short training courses. Training of additional staff
for the project's OFWM program would be conducted mainly in the field by
existing staff. Additionally, to improve the environmental monitoring



-19-
capability required under LBOD-Stage I (para 7.09), officers of the Sind
Department of Forestry and Wildlife associated with the Rann of Kutch
Wildlife Sanctuary would receive training as required.
Monitoring and Evaluation (base cost US$3.0 M)
3.21    Three separate but coordinated M&E activities would be carried out
during the project's development period: (a) day-to-day monitoring of
LBOD-Stage I physical and financial progress, including TW performance as TWs
are installed; (b) monitoring of physical parameters, including meteorologi-
cal data, depth to watertable, drain discharge, water quality, soil salinity,
and environmental effects; and (c) impact evaluation on beneficiaries in
terms of agricultural production, income and employment levels, economic
well-being of small farmers, and secondary effects in the regional economy.
The impact evaluation would be carried out over an 11-year period in order to
monitor the accretion of project benefits. In sequence, the evaluation study
would entail: detailed design, base-line survey, intermediate surveys and
selected studies, and, four years after completion of project works, a final
survey and evaluation report. M&E responsibilities are described in paras
5.14 and 5.15. Summary terms of reference for each of the activities are
presented in Annex 8.
Status of Designs
3.22    Final designs and draft tender documents have been completed for the
KPOD remodelling work, and designs are being finalized for DPOD's enlargement
and the KPOD/DPOD bifurcation structure, all of which comprise the first
year's work program under IDA financing. Most of the land has already been
acquired and active steps are underway to acquire any remaining small pieces
of land required for the first year's civil works to be financed by IDA.
Final designs also have been completed for the remainder of the work on the
spinal drain and structures currently being implemented by WAPDA with GOP
financing (core program). Feasibility studies have recently been completed
for the tidal link, Chotiari Reservoir, and Nara Canal remodelling. The
designs and estimates of the Jamrao Canal remodelling work are preliminary
and will be brought to final design level during the first two years of
project implementation. Final TW-field design and tile and interceptor drain
design for subsurface drainage will be initiated and carried out with the
assistance of project consultants following their appointment in early 1985.
IV. COST ESTIMATES. FINANCING. AND PROCUREMENT
Cost Estimates
4.01    Capital Costs.  Total project cost is estimated at about Rs 9,202.9 M
(US$635.7 M equivalent), including US$57.4 M for physical contingencies,
US$173.9 M for price escalation, and US$97.1 M for taxes and duties. Foreign
exchange costs are estimated to be about US$318.6 M, or about 50% of total
project costs. Base costs are expressed in September 1984 prices. Where
possible, cost estimates are based on unit prices taken from recent contracts
issued by WAPDA and on quotations from suppliers. Unit prices and quotations
have been adjusted to accommodate project conditions and to reflect current
cost levels.



-20-
4.02    O&M costs of completed works during project implementation (estimated
at about US$10.6 M) are also included in capital costs of the project.
Project costs include physical contingencies (10-20% of base costs) and price
contingencies based on projected local and international inflation rates.
Table 4.1 summarizes cost estimates and assumed inflation rates. More
detailed summary cost tables are presented in Annex 3, Tables 1-4. Additional
cost details for each project component are available in the project file.

4.03    Recurrent Costs After Proiect Completion.   By about year 2000, the
project's 0&M costs (including capital replacement) would average about
Rs 343.7 M (US$25 M) annually--about 6% of the project's initial capital cost
and equivalent to approximately Rs 670/ha of CCA (or Rs 600 per cropped ha)
over LBOD-Stage I at mid-1984 prices. However, O&M costs would vary con-
siderably among subareas because of physical differences, drainage and pump-
ing requirements, and related energy costs.
4.04    Over the project life of 40 years, estimated replacement costs
(including physical contingencies) would be about Rs 2,145 M (about 37% of
initial capital cost). From year 10 onward (or after about 1996), replace-
ment costs would average about Rs 70 M per year. At full development of
Stage I, about 2,000 additional personnel would be required, at an annual
cost of about Rs 29 M. Electricity, fuel, spare parts, and maintenance of
project works would total about Rs 244 M per year; power would comprise
nearly one-third of total project O&M costs. Total project O&M costs exclude



-21-
the costs of maintaining improved watercourses and perLodic relevelling of
fields, which would be carried out by farmers (para 5.06). Summary details
of the project's O&M costs for the main expenditure categories are presented
in Annex 3. Table 5.
FinancinR
4.05    Tentative project financing totalling about US$417.1 M, or about 77%
of project cost net of taxes and duties, has been agreed upon by 7 external
cofinanciers. This amount would finance about 93Z of foreign exchange costs
and 55% of local costs (net of taxes and duties). These cofinanciers are:
the International Development Association (IDA); Asian Development Bank
(ADB); Saudi Fund for Development (SF); Canadian International Development
Agency (CIDA); Overseas Development Administration (ODA-UK); Swiss Develop-
ment Corporation (SDC); and the OPEC Fund for Development. Table 4.2 sum-
marizes the tentative project financing plan, according to cofinancier and
respective contributions toward local and foreign costs. Annex 3, Tables 6-7
provide financing details according to project component. IDA's credit of
US$150 M equivalent comprises about 282 of total project cost (net of taxes
and duties). The financing arrangements of the various external agencies
also have been agreed in principle, subject to the review and approval of
their respective managements and boards; at this time, some of the cofinan-
ciers already have approved formally their contribution. The agreed financ-
ing plan provides for parallel (or single source) financing of most com-
ponents; joint financing is proposed for the OFWM, electrification (partial),
technical assistance and training, and M&E components. Remaining project
costs (US$218.6 M, inclusive of about US$97 M in taxes and duties) would be
financed by GOP. GOP has approached the Islamic Development Bank (IDB) to
finance US$15-20 M of the electrification investments. In the event such
funds are secured, GOP's contribution would decrease by such an amount.
Farmer-donated labor (or cash payments by farmers for substituted labor) for
watercourse renovation (totalling approximately US$1.0 M) has been excluded
from LBOD-Stage I cost estimates.

4.06    External funds obtained by GOP would be made available to GOSind and
WAPDA for project implementation through budgetary allocations. Existing GOP
procedures would be followed to fund project expenditures. WAPDA would
receive funds from the federal Annual Development Program (ADP) for all



-22-
expenditures on drainage works, electrification, Chotiari Reservoir, techni-
cal assistance, and M&E (amounting to about 75% of total cost). GOSind would
use funds from its provincial ADP for OFWM and the Nara and Jamrao canal
remodelling works (about 25% of total costs). The project's estimated expen-
diture schedule would be consistent with the allocations shown in the Sixth
Five-Year Plan (FY84-88); the project's remaining expenditures (or 70% of
total) would be made during the Seventh Five-Year Plan period (FY89-93).
Approval of the Umbrella 11 PC-1 for all LBOD-Stage I would be required ftom
the following: (a) on an anticipatory basis, by the Minister of Finance as
Chairman of the Executive Committee of the National Economic Council (ECNEC)
as a condition of Board presentation; and (b) by ECNEC as a condition of
credit effectiveness. A further condition of credit effectiveness would be
that all conditions precedent to the effectiveness of the other external
financing agencies be met. Assurances were obtained from GOP and GOSind that
necessary resources and funds to execute the project in accordance with the
implementation and expenditure schedules would be provided promptly as
required.
4.07    Special Account.  A special or imprest account would be set up for
funding of the following items financed by IDA: drainage, OFWM, electrifica-
tion, and canal remodelling works. IDA financing would be channeled through
the State Bank of Pakistan to three separate subaccounts established, respec-
tively, for WAPDA (drainage works and electrification), GOSind's Department
of Irrigation (canal remodelling works), and GOSind's Department of
Agriculture (OFWM works and staff costs). The WAPDA subaccount would be
administered by the Chief Engineer, IM, and GOSind's two subaccounts would
be administered by the respective departmental secretaries, under the overall
supervision of GOSind's Finance Secretary. The special account and sub-
accounts would operate as follows: (a) IDA, as requested by GOP/WAPDA/GOSind
immediately following credit effectiveness, under the overall coordination of
the IMI, and in accordance with the estimated expenditure schedule, would
deposit initially a level of rupees in the special subaccounts to cover about
four months projected expenditures; (b) subsequently, the subaccount would be
replenished monthly based on the monthly reimbursement applications received
from IM0 for the various items financed under the Credit; (c) as overall
coordinator for project execution, the IM0 would instruct the State Bank of
Pakistan to transfer the appropriate funds to the subaccount of the respec-
tive agency; IMO would also periodically consolidate all subaccounts (see
Annex 4 for procedures for operating the special account and subaccounts).
Although most withdrawals from the proceeds of the proposed credit account
and funds administered by IDA would be handled through the special account,
IDA would continue to accept, in appropriate cases, withdrawal applications
for direct payment and agreements to reimburse. An assurance was obtained
from GOP that a special account would be set up and operated in accordance
with guidelines agreed by IDA. Assurances also were obtained from WAPDA and
GOSind that special subaccounts would be set up and operated according to IDA
guidelines. The establishment of the special account would be a condition of
credit effectiveness.
1/ The Umbrella PC-1 would be comprised of the components for each of the
three subareas, including the spinal drain, tidal outfall, irrigation
works and OFWM.

Procurement
4.08    Most of the project works, representing about US$284.2 M equivalent
(or 53Z of project costs net of taxes and duties), would be procured accord-
ing to various international competitive bidding (ICB) procedures. Procure-
ment arrangements for civil works and goods and services would follow methods
agreed between GOP and each of the cofinanciers. Components (except OFWM)
financed by more than one cofinancier (US$92 M equivalent) would be separated
geographically to enable parallel financing and to meet the procurement
requirements of each financier. Para 4.09 highlights arrangements for
procurement of civil works and goods and services to be financed out of the
proceeds of the IDA credit. Procurement arrangements are shown in Table 4.3.
Detailed procurement implementation schedules have been prepared according to
project works and component items.
4.09    Civil works associated with the drainage system (KPOD and DPOD) and
canal remodelling would follow ICB procedures, with all labor, materials, and
equipment to be supplied by the contractors as part of the contract. The
electrification works undertaken by IDA would follow IDA's ICB procedures.
The OFWM works, which are not suitable for ICB because they are small and
scattered, would be carried out under small contracts awarded in accordance
with local competitive bidding procedures (LCB) satisfactory to IDA or force
account, as well as with farmer contributed labor organized by the WUAs. The
appointment of local supervisory consultants for the OFWM component would be
in accordance with IDA guidelines. Other consultants and training would be
funded on a joint basis by IDA and ADB. These consultants would be interna-
tionally selected and engaged by WAPDA on terms and conditions acceptable to
IDA and ADB. The selected international consulting firm would be encouraged
to associate with a local firm.
4.10    The relatively small amount of office and survey equipment required
for the OFWK component would be procured following GOP/GOSind procedures
satisfactory to IDA comparing prices from at least three independent sources.
The aggregate amount of such locally procured equipment would not exceed
US$0.3 M equivalent. Work contracts over US$1,000,000 would be subject to
IDA review prior to award. Other contracts would be subject to selective
post-award review. A preference margin of 7.5% on ICB civil works contracts
would be extended to qualified local contractors in bid evaluation under ICB.
