Disbursements
4.11    Disbursements from the IDA Credit would cover the indicated percent-
age of total expenditures for each item, including taxes and duties:
(a) Civil Works:
(i) KPOD and DPOD -- 80%
(ii) OFNM -- 42Z
(iii) Electrification -- 74%
(iv) Nara Canal and Jamrao Remodelling -- 80X
(b)  Office and Survey Equipment:  60% of local expenditures
(c)  Administration:
Incremental salaries and operating costs for OFWM, including
supervisory consultants -- 50%.
(d) Technical Assistance and Training: 37Z
4.12    Disbursements for (a) [except for item (ii)l, (c), and (d) for
contracts US$1.0 M equivalent or higher would be fully documented. Disburse-
ments for items (a)(ii) and (b) and civil work contracts below US$1.0 M
equivalent would be made against statement of expenditures. Supporting
documents would be retained by the implementing agencies for review by IDA.
Reimbursement applications for expenditures to be funded through the special
account would be accompanied by a statement of transaction on the special
account since the previous application, with the balance certified by the
State Bank of Pakistan. It is anticipated that the final disbursement would
be completed by December 31, 1993, about six months after project completion.
The disbursement schedule is based on the disbursement profile of
IDA-supported irrigation and drainage projects in Pakistan and on the use of
the special account (para. 4.07) which is expected to expedite the disburse-
ments under the Credit. Although most of these projects are in the early
stages of implementation and are not fully comparable, the proposed phasing
of project expenditures and scheduling of disbursements appear reasonable.
The disbursement schedule for IDA financing is shown in Annex 3, Table 8.
Accounts and Audits
4.13    The implementing units of WAPDA (South) and GOSind would maintain
separate project accounts. Directors of the subareas and of major project
components would submit accounts monthly to a specially formed Integrated
Management Organization (IMO) for review and consolidation, after which they
would be audited by independent auditors and submitted to IDA. The Audit
Report would include, inter alia, a statement verifying that funds advanced
by IDA to the special account and disbursed against statements of expenditure
and full documentation had been used for the purpose for which they were
provided. Assurances were obtained from GOP and GOSind that: (a) WAPDA
(South) and GOSind would prepare and maintain separate project accounts;
(b) IMO would prepare and maintain consolidated project accounts, including
records of the three special subaccounts; (c) the project accounts would be
audited annually by an independent auditor satisfactory to IDA; (d) copies of
the unaudited annual project account would be sent to IDA for review no later



-26-
than six months after the end of each fiscal year, and a copy of the final
auditor's report no later than nine months after the end of each fiscal year;
and (e) the auditor's report would include, inter alia, a statement that the
funds advanced by IDA for the special account and against statements of
expenditure had been used for the purpose for which they were provided.
V. PROJECT ORGANIZATION. MANAGEMENT. AND IMPLEMENTATION
Institutional Arrangements
5.01    In accordance with established procedures in Pakistan, WAPDA would be
responsible for implementing all aspects of the project except for Nara and
Jamrao Canal remodelling work and OFWM, which would be the responsibility of
GOSind's Department of Irrigation and the OFWM Directorate in the Department
of Agriculture, respectively. A Project Steering Committee (PSC) would be
established to provide overall guidance and coordination for the project
during implementation and the IMO would direct project implementation
activities and coordinate and integrate the work of the concerned implement-
ing agencies. The establishment of the PSC and IM0 by GOP and GOSind with
powers and functions satisfactory to IDA would be conditions of credit effec-
tiveness.
5.02    Proiect Steering Committee.  Through IMO, the PSC would ensure
adequate flexibility of project implementation, in light of additional data
obtained from pilot studies and early implementation works. Broader PSC
functions would include review and formulation of a cost-effective and ade-
quate O&M system and cost recovery actions. The PSC would be composed of
the ranking officials of WAPDA at the federal level and GOSind's Departments
of Irrigation, Agriculture, Planning and Development, and Finance. The
Additional Chief Secretary of the Planning and Development Department would
be Chairman of the PSC, and the General Manager (Water), WAPDA (South), would
be a member and Executive Secretary. Each member would ensure prompt provi-
sion of agency inputs necessary for the project or its related activities.
The PSC would meet quarterly or more often, as required, to: (a) ensure
coordinated scheduling of complementary activities by the implementing agen-
cies; (b) review and approve budgetary allocations proposed by the IMO for
each project component; and (c) review quarterly and annual progress reports.
5.03    InteRrated Management Organization.  Management and coordination of
project implementation would be undertaken by the IMO, headed by a Chief
Engineer and supported by qualified and full-time technical and administra-
tive staff. Consultants engaged under the project would assist the INO as
required. IMO would be subdivided into seven units, each headed by a Project
Director and suitably staffed with full-time, qualified supporting staff
according to normal procedures in WAPDA and the Sind Departments of Irriga-
tion and Agriculture (Annex 5, Chart I). An assurance was obtained from
GOSind and WAPDA that the staff and local consultants required for project
execution (if and when necessary) would be provided on a timely basis and,
specifically, key IMO staff required for PYI implementation would be
appointed by April 1, 1985. The Chief Engineer would be responsible to the
General Manager (Water), WAPDA (South). Project Directors and staff in all
Units, except the OFWM and Nara and Jamrao Units, would be principally WAPDA
staff, although at the professional level some of these staff would be
seconded from the Irrigation Department to facilitate a smooth transition of



-27-
the completed works to the Department for O&M (para 5.10). The Chief
Engineer, IMD, would coordinate technical and project implementation matters
for OFWM and Nara and Jamrao remodelling work, but the Directors for these
components would be administratively responsible to GOSind's Departments of
Agriculture and Irrigation, respectively. Project offices would be estab-
lished at Nawabshah, Sanghar, and Mirpurkhas, each headed by a full-time
Project Director from WAPDA who would have the rank of Superintending
Engineer. Each project office would be established in accordance with the
phasing of implementation of proiect components. In coordination with
GOSind, IMO would develop suitable procedures for transferring drainage works
and associated O&M to the Irrigation Department about one year after comple-
tion of construction in a given subarea; this transfer would include staff
and maintenance equipment provided under the project.
5.04    The IMO would be responsible for:  (a) overall planning and monitor-
ing of project implementation and the preparation of integrated annual work
programs. The annual work programs should be completed by February 1 of each
year for all project components, for review and approval by the PSC;
(b) preparation of design criteria, specifications, tender documents and
contracts, and procurement of all civil works and equipment;
(c) investigations and detailed designs for drainage TWs, scavenger and
special design TWs, tile drains, field drains, and interceptor drains;
Cd) collection of topographic, soils, land use and other data for detailed
designs; (e) coordinating the monitoring of surface and drainage flows in
the project area, changes in vatertable levels, well discharge, and
piezometric water levels in pumped aquifers; (f) coordinating the monitoring
of the effects of changes in water supply on land use and agricultural
production; (g) development of irrigation/drainage models for improving O&M
standards for completed systems; (h) coordinating the training of WAPDA and
GOSind staff; (i) construction of offices/quarters in the Wildlife Sanctuary
and outfall area for monitoring staff of the Department of Forestry and
Wildlife; (j) consolidating the project accounts, including the special
subaccounts, of the various implementing agencies and units and preparing
monthly reimbursement applications; (k) preparing quarterly and annual
progress reports from information furnished by the Project Directors, includ-
ing environmental reporting; and (1) preparing a project completion report
within six months of the close of the project.
5.05    A Project Director, Central Design and Monitoring, would prepare
plans, layouts, detailed engineering designs, and tender documents for con-
struction and would be responsible for carrying out and coordinating project
monitoring activities. Project Directors of the various implementing units
would: (a) prepare detailed implementation schedules for civil works,
contracts and procurement; (b) supervise construction of civil works;
(c) certify quantity and quality of work completed; (d) operate and maintain
completed works for the first year until O&M responsibility is transferred to
the Irrigation Department (for drainage civil works only); (e) maintain
project accounts; and (f) furnish monthly progress reports to the Chief
Engineer. The Project Director, OFNW, would liaise between the Chief
Engineer, IMO, and the OFWM Directorate of the GOSind's Department of
Agriculture and would be responsible for: (a) planning and design of the
OFWM program, including watercourse renovation, precision land levelling and
improved OFWM; (b) layout and construction of OFWM civil works; (c) estab-
liabment of demonstration plots; (d) supervision of waterco-urse O&M; and
(e) organization, registration, and guidance of WUAs. An assurance was



-28-
obtained from GOSind that training of at least two additional OFWM field
teams would be completed no later than April 1, 1985 to ensure the start of
expanded OFWM activities as soon as project funds become available.
5.06    Water Users' Associations.  Prior to undertaking any work on water-
courses to be renovated, formal WUAs would be organized under the authority
of the Sind Water Users' Associations Ordinance, 1982. Each WUA would be
registered in the office of the Director of GOSind's OFWM Directorate. WUAs
are authorized to install, operate and maintain watercourse improvements and
drainage facilities, establish water delivery schedules, execute contracts,
and, if necessary, acquire land. The main purpose of WUAs is to organize
farmers' contributions of labor and cash for OFNM on a watercourse basis. An
assurance was obtained from GOSind that the organization of formal WUAs would
be a pre-condition to farmers receiving OFWM assistance. An assurance also
was obtained that GOSind would undertake a phased program to extend the WUA
functions to drainage O&M and would make necessary amendments to the Water
Users' Association Ordinance of 1982 no later than July 1, 1986 to cover all
aspects of drainage, including the construction and maintenance of farm
drains.
Proiect Implementation
5.07    The project would be implemented over eight years (FY86-FY93), as
shown in Annex 5, Chart 3. During project year one (PY1), the ongoing con-
struction of the spinal drain is expected to be completed up to RD 820 in
Nawabshah, and the enlargement of KPOD and DPOD (to be carried out over a
three-year period) would commence. Final designs and draft tender documents
for these works have been completed, and WAPDA is expected to call tenders
for KPOD/DPOD construction work immediately following credit effectiveness in
order tc facilitate early implementation. The construction of surface drains
in Nawabshah and Sanghar and of interceptor drains and 11 kV distribution
lines in Nawabshah (expected to be completed in 3-3 1/2 years) would also be
initiated during PY1, as would OFWM activities in Nawabshah. Drainage TW
construction, installation of 11 kV distribution lines, and OFWM activities
in Sanghar would start in PY2. Interceptor drains in Sanghar, surface drains
in Mirpurkhas, and tidal link construction would follow in PY3, and OFWM
would be introduced in Nirpurkhas in PY4. The construction of Chotiari
Reservoir and the remodelling of Nara Canal, starting in mid-PY3 and to be
carried out over 3 years, would precede the construction of the Jamrao Canal
remodelling work (spanning 4 years), starting in PY4. Tile drains and inter-
ceptor drains in Mirpurkhas would be among the last components to be imple-
mented, starting in PY5. Maintenance facilities would be provided according
to the timing requirements of the project, starting in PY3.
5.08    In order to coordinate the timely implementation of the various
project activities, in PYI _.onsultants would assist IMO staff in setting up
the organization for overall planning and monitoring of project implementa-
tion and in initiating investigations, completing feasibility studies, and
preparing detailed design criteria and final designs and layouts for drainage
and scavenger TWs (including special-design TWs), tile drains, interceptor
and surface drains, and the tidal link. During the first two years of
project implementation, the irrigation suitability land classification inves-
tigations, carried out earlier, would be refined as part of completing the
final drainage designs. Criteria of economic evaluation would be applied to
both drainage investigations and land classification surveys. The major



-29-
emphasis would be on the identification and delineation of irrigable and
non-irrigable land, so that the lands to be reclaimed would viably maintain
agricultural enterprise and justify investment. Concurrently, small-scale
pilot schemes would be implemented during PY1 to test and finalize the most
efficient designs for scavenger TWs and drainage TWB in fine-grained
aquifers, before the start of full-scale installation of these wells in PY3
and PY4. Feasibility studies and implementation plans for Chotiari Reservoir
and Nara/Jamrao Canal remodelling would also be completed during the first
two years of the project. Since technical assistance for design and
implementation would be required from the beginning of the project, WAPDA has
started bid invitation procedures for the required consulting services.
Assurance was obtained from WAPDA that the appointment and fielding of the
consultants would be completed no later than July 1, 1985. An assurance was
obtained from GOSind that supervisory consultants for OFWM would be appointed
no later than January 1, 1987. Assurances also were obtained from GOSind and
WAPDA that arrangements for staff training would be satisfactory to IDA.
Operation and Maintenance
5.09    IMO would be responsible for the O&M of all completed drainage works
during project implementation and continuing about one year after the works
become fully operational, after which O&M responsibilities would be trans-
ferred to GOSind's Irrigation Department. An effective communication system
would be established to control irrigation releases from Chotiari Reservoir
and the operation of the drainage TWs. Meteorological data and information
would be used to anticipate rainstorms and other events which influence
irrigation releases and pumping schedules. The existing hydrometeorological
network would be augmented to obtain the information required. Distribution
of drainage flow between the KPOD and DPOD outfall channels would be
automatic, being incorporated in the hydraulic design of the bifurcation
structure. WAPDA Power (South) would operate and maintain the electrifica-
tion works constructed under the project as part of the existing national
generation, transmission, and distribution facilities.
5.10    Drainage system maintenance would fall into two general categories:
(a) drain channel and subsurface drain maintenance, including the outfall;
and (b) TW field maintenance. Past standards and funds for O&M of irrigation
channels, SCARP TWs, and surface drains in Sind have generally been inade-
quate, resulting in deterioration of the system and gradual reduction in
efficiency. A considerably strengthened O&M organization would therefore be
set up in the IMO, with adequate facilities, staff, and equipment to carry
out O&M during project implementation and one year beyond construction com-
pletion (Annex 5, Chart 2). The staff, equipment, and facilities would be
transferred to a Drainage and Maintenance Circle in GOSind's Irrigation
Department one year after subarea or project completion. Assurances were
obtained from GOSind that Provincial O&M personnel would be seconded to the
project on O&M assignment for at least one year prior to completion of works
in a given subarea to ensure a smooth transition in the handover of O&M
responsibilities. Assurances were also obtained from GOSind and WAPDA that
at the completion of construction and again prior to overall project handover
to GOSind, a joint inspection of the drainage and irrigation water storage
facilities would be made by key O&M personnel from IMO, GOSind, and WAPDA to
identify deficiencies and agree on corrective actions, including delineating
responsibility and developing a schedule for executing necessary repairs.
The inspection results would be documented in a detailed handover report.



-30-
The use of microcomputers would be introduced on a pilot basis for O&M
scheduling, compilation and analysis of data, financial accounting, inventory
control, and O&M cost analysis. The O&M program would include establishment
and maintenance of necessary roads and right-of-ways, which are critical to
effective O&M. Considerable emphasis would be placed on on-the-job and other
training for O&M staff at all levels throughout project implementation. The
training of OM personnel would include operation, maintenance and management
seminars, direct discussion, and on-site visits. Since weed growth is a
major problem, staff would be trained in adequate control methods. Additional
details on the operational plan for O&M of the drainage system are given in
Annex 14 of the Project Preparation Report, which is available in the project
file.
5.11    To guard against long-term deterioration of the system, GOSind,
assisted by WAPDA, would designate an independent team to monitor and review
at three-year intervals O&M requirements of LBOD-Stage I in light of opera-
tional experience, to ascertain actual O&M needs of the drainage system and
modify procedures as necessary. The team would consist of 2-3 professional
specialists experienced in O&M and review procedures, in addition to having
expertise unique to specific areas and requirements. Such reviews would
follow the recommendations given in the "Study and Reasonable Requirement of
Funds for Operation and Maintenance of Canal Irrigation System, SCARPS and
Protective Bunds in Sind, 1979," for OM. A report, to be reviewed by IDA,
would be prepared describing the condition of the system, adequacy of the O&M
program, and deficiencies to be corrected. Annual reviews also would be made
by project operations personnel to evaluate facilities, set priorities, and
prepare O&M budgets for the following year. Assurances were obtained from
GOSind and WAPDA that during project implementation and thereafter an inde-
pendent team acceptable to IDA would monitor and review at three-year inter-
vals drainage O&M requirements in the LBOD Stage I project area, starting no
later than January 1, 1988.
5.12    A detailed operations manual would be prepared by project operation
staff with the assistance of the consultants. It would inclusively outline
the overall and day-to-day O0M requirements and procedures for establishing
and implementing a preventive maintenance program. An assurance was obtained
from WAPDA that operational procedures would be developed in greater detail
by the IMO and that the draft OM manual would be prepared and submitted to
IDA for review and comment no later than January 1, 1988 and sequentially
updated as project components are completed, with a final report no later
than January 1, 1991.
5.13    Funding for OM of the drainage system (including the spinal
drain), when taken over by GOSind, would be provided from the provincial
non-development budget. It would be prudent to establish a capital replace-
ment fund to ensure available funds for replacement of major items at the end
of their useful life. An assurance was obtained from GOSind that adequate
and timely funds would be made available annually for full O&M requirements
(including capital replacement costs) to maintain acceptable O&M standards,
as determined by periodic reviews described in para 5.11, to ensure opera-
tional efficiency of the drainage system over the long term. IMO would
retain responsibility for O&M of the spinal drain until all component
sub-drainage systems provided under LBOD-Stage I are completed and fully
operational.

Monitoring and Evaluation
5.14    IHO and GOSind would have overall responsibility for the M&E informa-
tion system required by LBOD-Stage 1. IMO would arrange to gather informa-
tion on day-to-day project activities, in order to provide early warning of
bottlenecks that may emerge during project execution. The monitoring of
physical parameters (para. 3.21) would be integrated with the ongoing
data-gathering program of WAPDA's M&E Organization; this program would be
expanded to meet the project's monitoring requirements. On the basis of
agreed terms of reference, an evaluation study would be contracted to
suitable and independent local institution. The local institution selected
would contract with an acceptable international institution for the services
of an agricultural economist/farm management specialist, an agronomist, a
statistician, and other experts as required to fulfill the agreed terms of
reference, particularly those related to the detailed design of the study and
benchmark, intermediate, and final surveys and the analysis and report writ-
ing. These experts would also be expected to emphasize on-the-job training
of local personnel and thereby contribute to the project's institution-
building objectives. The selection of the consultants would be in accordance
with IDA procedures acceptable to the other participating donors. The final
evaluation report would be due in mid-1997, about four years after completion
of project works. The long project implementation period and the longer
period required for effective evaluation place a high premium on the quality
of the evaluation design, continuity and consistency in its execution, and
minimal changes in the associated personnel. GOSind's Planning and Develop-
ment Department, in close coordination with the IMO, would be the executing
agency for the evaluation study.
5.15    Assurances were obtained from WAPDA that:  (a) IMO would prepare and
send to IDA for comment by September 30, 1985 a detailed monitoring and
reporting program, including schedule of activities organization, staff
responsibilities and costs; and (b) a memorandum of understanding would be
concluded by February 1, 1986 between IMO and WAPDA's M&E Organization to
monitor the project's related physical parameters based on an agreed York
program. Assurances were obtained from GOSind that: (a) GOSind's Department
of Planning and Development, in collaboration with the IMO, would prepare and
furnish to IDA for comment terms of reference and invitations for proposals
from independent local institutions for project impact evaluation by
September 30, 1985; and (b) a contract would be concluded by Februar) 1, 1986
with a suitable local institution acceptable to IDA to carry out the
project's evaluation study under terms of reference and conditions sa:isfac-
tory to IDA. The local institution selected would also complete a sub-
contract with a suitable international institution by February 1, 1986 to
assist in carrying out the evaluation study in accordance with the agreed
terms of reference, including assistance in preparation of a project comple-
tion report within 6 months of the close of the project. Terms of reference
and implementation schedule and arrangements for the M&E component are sum-
marized in Annex 8.

VI.  PRODUCTION. MARKETING AND PRICES. FARM INCOMES. AND COST RECOVERY
Agricultural Impact
6.01    The project would increase agricultural production over about 516,000
ha of CCA by lowering watertables and salinity levels, saving or recovering
water lost from the irrigation system, and providing supplementary irrigation
waters.
6.02    Notwithstanding the difficulties in predicting precisely the agricul-
tural impact, estimates under scenarios of "with" and "without" the project
have been made. Based on findings from field surveys and complemented by
data from comparable areas elsewhere, they have been subjected to quantita-
tive analysis. 1/ The project's main agricultural assumptions and impact in
the Stage I area are outlined below.
6.03    Land Use and Cropping Intensities.  On the basis of observed
relationships between water supply and cropped area and between watertable
depth and the proportion of fallow/abandoned land in the uncropped area, land
uses "with" and '"ithout" the project have been projected. The main conclu-
sions for full LBOD-Stage I development are as follows:
Without Project: Assuming additional water from Kalabagh and Rohri canal
remodelling, average annual cropping intensity would increase from about
81% at present to about 96%. Farming intensity (Annex 7, Table 1) would
rise, contributing to higher watertables and a subsequent reduction in
y.elds.
With Project: Including proposed increases in water supplies (Annex 7,
Table 1), particularly in Sanghar and Mirpurkhas, the overall annual
cropping intensity would increase to about 117X -- about 22% higher than
without project. Given the continuing shortage of irrigation water, it
is expected that farmers would use most of the additional water supplies
to increase cropping intensities and would continue their current
(profitable) practice of water spreading. Although farming intensity
would exceed cropping intensity levels at present or without the project,
fallow land would increase and abandoned land would be reduced. Annex 7,
Tables 1-2 include data on cropping intensities for the three subareas
and Stage I overall.
6.04    Croppin2 Pattern.  With the project, cultivated areas of the dominant
crops - cotton and wheat -- would rise, because of the crops' relative
profitability, as more water supplies and drainage are provided (Table 6.1,
and Annex 7, Tables 1-2). The project area is one of Pakistan's best
/ The Indus Basin Model, developed in the late 1970s by the Bank's Develop-
ment Research Department in collaboration with WAPDA, was adopted and
used for facilitating the evaluation of project design optioas and
impact. Based on farm -level data collected in the project area and
observed relationships of key parameters, the modelling work included
the interaction between crop and livestock activities and an adjustment
for risk. Additional details on the model as adapted for LBOD-Stage I,
are available in the project file.



-33-
cotton-growing areas, and project improvements are expected to further con-
solidate its comparative advantage. If the shift toward more diversified
high-value crops (such as fruit orchards and vegetables) is greater than
projected, an even higher value of production would be generated.
6.05    Yields.  Project-induced incremental yields among crops are expected
to vary, ranging from about 6Z-55% (Table 6.1, and Annex 7, Tables 1-2).
With a lowering of the high watertables that occur during kharif, higher
yields are expected for kharif crops than for rabi crops. With the project,
yield levels are expected to follow generally upward trends observed since
about 1950. Even under current conditions, average crop yields would con-
tinue to rise, despite high watertables, as a result of increased and effi-
cient usage of agricultural inputs, better cultivation practices, and
improved support services. Yield increases would be suppressed, however, by
the combined effects of waterlogging, soil salinity, intensive cropping, and
stormwater damage (Annex 7, Table 3). The underlying assumptions of
crop-yield response to watertable depth and of the percentage of area with
high watertables in the absence of drainage are shown in Annex 7, Tables
4a-4b. Without drainage, any significant increase in irrigation water would
result in even higher watertables, increased salinization of uncropped land,
and, consequently, in less fallow land and lower yields because of water-
logging and salinity. In Sanghar and Nirpurkhas, particularly, the situation
is approaching the point where any benefits of increased irrigation supplies
on the cropped area would be outweighed by loss of yields. Adequate
drainage, however, would enable crop yields to reach their full potential and
would justify further investments in utilizing available irrigation supplies.

6.06    Production.  Project-induced increases in cropping intensities and
yields are expected to generate substantial incremental production, par-
ticularly of cotton and wheat. At full development of LBOD-Stage I, an
additional 314,800 metric tons (mt) of seed cotton and 214,500 mt of wheat
would be produced. As shown in Table 6.1, moderate production increases in
fodder, oilseeds, sugarcane, and mangoes are also expected. A modest
increase in meat production would occur, reflecting an increased livestock
population resulting from the project's higher cropping intensities. Further
details on the agricultural impact for each subarea and SLage I overall are
provided in Annex 7, Tables 1-2.



-34-
Marketinz and Pricin-
6.07    Current marketing, processing, and pricing arrangements for the
project-s agricultural inputs and outputs are satisfactory and are not likely
to impose constraints on achieving projected production and farmer-income
levels. These activities are carried out increasingly by the private sector,
with varying degrees of government intervention in marketing and pricing of
selected crops.
6.08    Currently, about 60% of Pakistan-s wheat production is used for
on-farm consumption. The remaining 40Z is sold to government procurement
centers and private wholesalers and millers. Government-procured wheat is
sold at the official price to authorized mills. Restrictions on the
transport of wheat between provinces have recently been removed. The wheat
production targets of the current Five-Year Plan assume gradual increases in
both domestic consumption and wheat exports. Therefore, increased wheat
production resulting from the project is expected to be readily marketed.
Seed cotton is sold either directly or through an intermediary to ginneries
and mills. The project area has about 50 ginneries, some with underutilized
capacity.  Part of the ginned cotton is consumed domestically; the remaining
cotton lint is exported by the Pakistan Export Corporation. A growing local
and export market for Pakistan's high-quality cotton is expected to provide
an adequate marketing outlet for the project's output, which, although sub-
stantial, would still represent only a small proportion of Pakistan's overall
cotton production. Three authorized sugar mills are located in the Nawabshah
and Mirpurkhas subareas. Farmers located near the mills usually market cane
directly and are paid the prevailing procurement price, based on the cane's
sucrose content. Some mills operating at full capacity plan to expand their
plant, depending on potential supplies and markets. Export prospects are not
bright because of current low international prices and the high cost of
production in Pakistan. However, the modest incremental production expected
under the project would easily be absorbed by increased domestic demand, and
planned increases in processing capacity in the project area would be ade-
quate to meet the increased requirements.
6.09    Other project outputs -  oilseeds, fodder, mangoes, and livestock
products - would increase only slightly and would be absorbed by farm-level
consumption and surrounding local markets, as well as the major urban centers
of Hyderabad and Karachi, Pakistan's largest and fastest-growing.
6.10    Agricultural prices in Pakistan are influenced to varying degrees by
GOP policy intervention through importlexport controls, taxes, consumer/
producer subsidies, and fixed procurement prices for major crops, although
such interventions are being substantially and deliberately reduced. In
early 1980, GOP announced a reorientation of its agricultural pricing
policies, including: the phasing out of subsidies to inputs, particularly
irrigation O&M costs, fertilizers, and pesticides; providing adequ&te price
incentives to producers; and transferring agricultural marketing and dis-
tribution responsibilities to the private sector. Recoveries from water
charges in Pakistan have increased nearly 100% in nominal terms over the past
six years. Fertilizer prices have also increased steadily, beginning with a
50% increase in 1980; GOP's intent is to phase out fertilizer subsidies by
the mid-1980s. In Sind, pesticide subsidies were eliminated in 1982. GOP,
based on the recommendations of the recently established Agricultural Price



-35-
Commission, is adjusting procurement prices of major crops to ensure adequate
price incentives to farmers and to offset higher production costs stedmng
from the reduction and eventual removal of input subsidies. Since 1980,
output prices of most procurement crops have increased and are nov
approximating import/export prices of most tradable commodities. Recent
stagnation in international commodity price levels explains, for the most
part, negligible price increases in the past year or two. As market prices
recover, GOP would be able to increase agricultural output prices, and is
likely to rely increasingly oa. market determinants.
6.11    The input and output prices used for the project's financial analysis
are summarized in Annex 7, Table 5. All prices are expressed in mid-1984
values. Present prices are based on the latest GOP procurement and support
prices, field-survey results during project preparation, and mission find-
ings. Future prices reflect GOP's declared policy of phasing out subsidies
and of adjusting both input and output prices of tradable commodities in line
with world market prices as projected by the World Bank, adjusted for market-
ing and handling, and processing costs to the farm-gate level (Annex 7,
Tables 6-7 show these adjustments for the main outputs of seed cotton and
wheat). Future prices for nontradables and other items are maintained at
present price levels (mid-1984 currency values).
Farm Models and Incomes
6.12    The direct impact on increased agricultural production and farmer
incomes would depend on: future watertable depth and its effect on crop
yields; irrigation water supplies and resulting cropping intensities; and
land tenure arrangements affecting the distribution of benefits between
landowners and tenant farmers. Five illustrative farm models have been
prepared to assess project impact on farmers' production, income levels, and
incentive to participate in the project, and also to assess farmers' capacity
to repay a portion of the project-s costs (para 6.15). Each model is
designed to reflect a reasonable variation of the above determinants,
including relatively low, average, and high watertable conditions, and to
illustrate the range of incremental benefits that would be generated
throughout the project area. The models assume average farm and family size,
cropping pattern, and input usage, and prevalent cultivation practices. Four
of the five models illustrate the project's impact on tenant farmers, repre-
senting nearly 75Z of total farm units in the project area. Existing legal
protection granted to tenants and increased labor required by the project
jointly would ensure widespread continuation of tenant farming. The farm
models are based on agricultural assumptions outlined in paras 6.02-6.06;
summary results for crop budgets on a per-hectare basis are presented in
Annex 7, Table 10. Table 6.2 shows the summary results of the farm model
analysis. Annex 7, Tables 11-15 present additional details, including net
farm incomes, recoveries, and calculation of rent accruing to project
beneficiaries during the project's life (1985 to 2025) .1/
jL/ Rent represents net benefits adjusted for compensation to farm family
labor and management. Recoveries--water/drainage and other incremental
charges-are not included in the rent estimate and are shown separately.

6.13    "Without" the project, incomes are expected to rise gradually and
substantially over present low levels, which average about Rs 1,230 (US$91)
per capita a year for most farm families. This level is still below the
current estimate of absolute rural poverty in Pakistan (US$110 per capita
in mid-1984 prices). Moreover, in 25 years, per capita income levels for
at least 80% of the farmers in the project area would still be quite low --
about US$160, which is substantially below the current level of average per
capita income in Pakistan (about US$325 in mid-1984 constant prices).
"With" the project, farmers ultimately would derive substantial but variable
increases in their net farn income; they would range from 22% under condi-
tions of "low watertable and cropping intensity" to 118% under conditions of
"high watertable and cropping intcnsity" (Table 6.2, and Annex 7, Tables
11-15). The conditions represented by the "low" and "average" scenarios
prevail over a large area of Nawabshah and at the tail ends of the irrigation
system. The "high" watertable model typifies much of Sanghar and Mirpurkhas.
6.14    Upon completion of project works in a given subarea, incremental
benefits to farmers would begin to accrue; significant benefits for most
farmers would materialize by 1995. These benefits reflect the visible and
relatively immediate and combined effects of lowering high watertables and
raising cropping intensities. Thereafter, the build-up of benefits would
occur zere gradually until full development of LBOD-Stage I is reached
(Annex 7, Tables 11-15). Estimates of tenant net farm incomes reflect
prevailing arrangements whereby about 50% of the total net farm income is
shared with the landowner. The tenant's share represents an average return
of about Rs 104 per day worked or approximately four times the projected wage
rate for hired farm labor and twice the equivalent return to labor "without"
the project. All groups of farmers under the project, including the large
percentage of tenant farmers, are expected to reap substantial benefits from
the project, which would provide adequate incentives for farmer participation
in: (a) providing donated labor for watercourse improvements and 0&M; (b)
contributing to the project's cost recovery (paras 6.15-6.18); and (c) adopt-
ing improved farming practices to take advantage of the more favorable



-37-
production environment made possible by drainage and additional water
supplies.
Cost Recovery
6.15    GOSind's current objective is to increase both water and drainage
charges so that the full costs of both irrigation and drainage system O&M are
recovered in a phased manner from direct beneficiaries. Under agreements for
other Bank Group operations, jj full recovery of irrigation system expendi-
tures in Sind Province is expected to be reached by July 1, 1988. Total
irrigation system OM expenditures currently are estimated to be Rs 100 per
cropped ha. In FY84, GOSind also introduced a "drainage charge" in areas
currently provided with drainage, averaging about Rs 40 per cropped ha (or
about 20Z of current sub-optimal drainage O&M expenditures) in areas with
public TW facilities, and a nominal charge for areas with surface drainage
only. In addition, GOSind recovers cash costs of-and farmers supply donated
labor for-OFWM works.
6.16    Irrigation charges are collected on the basis of area cropped and
vary for different crops according to their water requirements. The deter-
mination of water charge assessments and collections for a specific area at
any time is therefore difficult. However, available aggregate data (Annex 7,
Table 16) show that water charges collected in Sind during 1984 averaged
about Rs 45 per ha of CCA, or about Rs 55 per cropped ha. Estimates for the
project area are somewhat higher (about Rs 60 per cropped ha), because of the
higher proportion of cotton grown. During the past four years through FY84,
GOSind has increased irrigation water charges by about 150% (or an average of
25% per year in nominal terms). Despite these substantial increases, in FY84
irrigation charges collected amounted to only about 44% of total irrigation
OM expenditures (Annex 7, Table 16). 2/  Collection arrangements for irriga-
tion charges function adequately, as the generally high collection rates
(about 95%), in comparison with other countries, attest.
6.17    No drainage charges currently are being collected in the LBOD-Stage
I project area. The drainage works for the first project subarea is expected
to be completed in 1989, with initial drainage charges to be collected in
about 1990. It is proposed that the initial recovery of combined charges for
irrigation and drainage OM for this subarea in 1990 be about Rs 245 per
cropped ha in 1984 prices (Rs 120 for irrigation and Rs 125 for drainage).
This amount would be about 300% higher than current recoveries of about Rs 60
per ha for irrigation only. Although full irrigation OM cost recovery of
Rs 120 per ha is expected to be 1988, drainage charges would continue to
increase periodically until the equivalent of full drainage O&M cost
recovery, currently estimated at over Rs 600 per cropped ha in 1984 prices
(Annex 3, Table 5), is achieved in about year 2005.
1/  Irrigation Systems Rehabilitation Project, Cr. 1239-PAK, Command Water
Management Project, Cr. 1487-PAK and Structural Adjustment Lending, Ln.
2166-PAR and Cr. 1255-PAK.
VJ  It should be noted that during the period FY80-84, irrigation 06&  expen-

are relatively high, compared to the current level. Detailed farm budget
analyses (presented in Annex 7, Tables 11-15), however, indicate that at
projected levels of expected benefits, direct beneficiaries would have the
capacity to repay total estimated project induced charges, including 100% of
full O&M costs. For example, based on the average farm unit, total incremen-
tal recoveries as a percentage of incremental farm income (before recoveries)
remains about 26% in 1990, 1995, and 2005 (Annex 7, Table 12).

6.19    Assurances were obtained from GOSind that it would:  (a) no later
than February 1, 1986, review with IDA the status and required actions to
achieve irrigation cost recovery by July 1, 1988 in accordance with covenants



-39-
previously obtained; (b)(i) about one year after commencement of full onera-
tion of the facilities in each sub-area, to collect drainage charges from the
beneficiaries to cover 25% of the O&M costs of the facilities; and (b)(ii)
thereafter, at least every two years, increase the drainage charges so as to
recover at least 50% of the O&M costs by July 1, 1995 and 100% of the O&M
costs by July 1, 2005, provided that the drainage charges keep pace and are
commensurate with the benefits actually accruing to the beneficiaries; and
(c) at least three months before the initial charge and subsequent increases,
to review with IDA the estimated actual farm benefits against the projected
benefits and proposed charges: and (d) require farmers benefitting from
watercourse improvements to contribute all unskilled labor and to repay a
portion (25x) of the cost of materials and skilled labor over five years in
accordance with existing arrangements (OFWM, Cr. 1163-PAK).
6.20    At full developme2t, total drainage O&M costs of LBOD-Stage I as
currently estimated would approximate Rs 340 N per year (1984 prices). This
equals about 70% of GOSind's total FY84 irrigation O&M expenditures (Annex 3,
Table 5), which highlights the magnitude of this project's drainage O&M
requirements. Actual O&M requirements would be periodically reviewed (paras
5.11-5.13). At full development (2010), the total charges (including ushr)
expected to be levied on farmers would equal about 20-40% of beneficiaries'
project rent, and 18-34Z of their incremental net farm income (Annex 7,
Tables 11-15), depending on varying farm conditions--primarily: farm size,
tenure arrangements, watertable conditions, and cropping intensities. Under
the current formal system, the owner or landlord is responsible for all water
and drainage charges and other levies. The above recovery percentages assume
a continuation of current practices whereby various informal arrangements
between tenants and landlords tend to result in a sharing of crop production
and charges. Total project direct recoveries in present value terms are
estimated to be about Rs 1,386 N, or about 23% of total project capital and
recurrent costs.
6.21    Despite the uncertainties in determining the actual levels of current
recoveries and of future levels based on realistic drainage O&M requirements
(to be determined by periodic reviews - para 5.11), the proposed phasing of
increased recovery rates is considered reasonable. These levels of proposed
recoveries reflect the relatively high cost of providing adequate drainage
0&M compared with 0&N costs of a surface irrigation system. This is
primarily due to high energy and replacement costs of operating project TWs
and drainage pumps. The repayment burden on farmers would be considerable,
and some resistance could be expected to the introduction of the initial
drainage charge when tangible benefits are only just beginning to accrue.
The farmers' repayment capacity would be continuously evaluated in the
project impact study provided under the M&E component. At the same time, the
collection of such a relatively high initial drainage charge shortly after
completing drainage works and increasing it periodically is considered essen-
tial to ensure adequate provincial resource mobilization to meet the
project's O&M requirements.



-40-
VII. BENEFITS. JUSTIFICATION. AND RISKS
Overview
7.01    The rationale underlying the project's large size and phasing ia
based on three main considerations. First, economies of scale associated
with the core drain require a relatively large area to be covered in order to
ensure a technically and economically viable project, whether or not future
LBOD stages are carried out. Second, of the 11 potential sub-areas in the
IBOD command area, the three sub-areas selected for the Stage I project are
most seriously in need of drainage and are contiguous to the main drain.
Third, the project option selected has the highest net present value within
financing constraints. The project would restore and enhance the agricul-
tural productivity of land and water resources over about 516,000 ha of CCA
in the LBOD-Stage I area, with the folloving annual incremental benefits:
o   Production:  314,800 mt of seed cotton, 214,500 mt wheat, and
moderate increases in other crop and livestock products (para 6.06);
o   Direct Beneficiaries:  about 910,000 persons, or 140,000 farm
families, plus approximately 120,000 beneficiaries of the project's
secondary benefits (paras 7.07-7.08);
o   Income Levels:  increases ranging from 22% to 118%, mostly for small
farmer families with annual incomes currently below US$100 per
capita; with project increases in income from the present to the year
2010 are equivalent to annual growth rates ranging from 3-5.5% in
real terms (Ta-ble 6.2 and para 6.13);
o   Employment:  about 47%, or an additional 42,000 man-years equivalent,
in addition to about 90,000 man-years equivalent of unskilled labor
during the eight-year project construction period (para 7.02);
o   Foreign Exchange:  about US$180 M equivalent per annum (para 7.04);
o   Non-Agricultural Primary Benefits:  reduced damage from stormwater
and high watertables to regional infrastructure in the amount of
about Rs 44 M per annum (para 7.06);
o   Secondary Benefits:  project-induced economic activity due to multi-
plier effects; an increase in direct value added in the agriculture
sector of Rs 1 N would result in a further Rs 1.4 N being generated
in the value added in the provincial economy (para 7.07); and
o Environmental Impact: significant improvements to, or at minimum
no material damage to, the environment in the project and surrounding
area (paras 7.09-7.12).
Employment and Income Effects
7.02    The current population of the project area is expected to double
during the project development period (1985-2010). 'Without" the project,
underemployment during slack periods, and the resulting depressed incomes,
would likely become a major socioeconomic problem. By contrast, the project
would generate additional farm labor requirements of about 42,000 man-years



-41-
equivalent, or a 47% higher demand than without the project. The project's
construction activities would also generate substantial employment oppor-
tunities during the implementation period, or a total of about 90,000
man-years equivalent (or an average of 9,000-12,000 man-years per annum) of
primarily unskilled labor. In addition, about 2,000 jobs would be generated
by the project's annual O&M activities. This increase in labor requirements
would absorb a major portion of the current underemployment among the rural
population fox most of the year and would provide the large tenant population
with a source of remunerative and reliable employment, even with the current
trend of mechanization. Availability of labor for both project construction
and increased on-farm activities, even during peak labor periods, is not
expected to constrain the project, especially because labor from surrounding
areas, also suffering from severe underemployment, can be drawn upon, if
necessary.
7.03    Following completion of project works in about 1993, approximately
140,000 farm families would benefit directly from the project through sub-
stantial increases in net farm incomes (paras 6.13-6.14). Approximately 70%
of the operational holdings are 5 ha or less in size and generate income
below the currently estimated level of absolute rural poverty. At full
development, the annual income of an average tenant farm of about 5 ha is
expected to increase substantially to about US$270 per capita as compared to
US$175 per capita without the project. About 30Z of the project area is
farmed by owner-cultivators and 70% is farmed by sharecropping tenants, who,
based on existing sharing arrangements of farm production and charges, would
receive about 50% of the net farm income. Based on this information, an
estimated 50% of the project-s total farm income benefits are estimated to
accrue to owner-cultivators and tenants having small holdings (e.g., 5 ha
or less). Moreover, tenants comprise nearly 75% of the project area s
population; low-income farmers, comprised primarily of tenants would there-
fore be among the project's main beneficiaries. Assuming current sharing
arrangements continue, the income distribution effect of the project is
likely to be neutral. There are two important safeguards to assuring that
tenants would reap these estimated benefits, or that they would not be
evicted after project improvements. First, tenants have informal and acquired
rights, some of which are regulated by legislation. It is expected that
after project improvements tenants would continue to be protected by their
existing rights. Without the project, land resources would continue to
deteriorate, which could lead landlords to evict some of the tenants in
accordance with landlord acquired rights. Second, the substantial project
generated farm employment requirements, even allowing for mechanization,
should also reduce the possibility of eviction and help ensure an equitable
return to the tenant.
Direct Foreign Exchange Effects
7.04    On the basis of projected international prices expressed in mid-1984
constant currency units, the annual gross foreign exchange value of incremen-
tal production of tradables generated by the project at full development is
estimated at about US$195 M, most of which would be derived from cotton and
wheat production. Adjusted for the foreign exchange costs (about US$15 M) of
traded inputs (mainly fertilizers), the project's identifiable direct annual
foreign exchange effect would total about US$180 M.



-42-
Economic Analysis
7.05    With incremental capital and recurrent project costs and benefits
valued in border rupees and constant currency values, the economy of Pakistan
is expected to realize total net benefits from project investments of about
Rs 2.2 billion (US$163 M) in present value terms. Pakistan's economy would
realize nearly a 14% economic rate of return from LBOD-Stage I investments;
rates of return estimated separately for each of the three main subareas
range from 12-22% and primarily reflect varying investment costs and, to a
lesser extent, variable agricultural benefits. Examination of various
project design options confirms that project viability and maximizing net
present value require careful phasing of irrigation and drainage-related
investments. Even after inclusion of the past expenditures on the spinal
drain, which are considered as justified sunk costs, the project is still
economically viable (Annex 7, Table 21). Table 7.1 summarizes the economic
results for each subarea and the Stage-I project overall.
