The economic cash flow for the three subareas and overall project are
provided in Annex 7, Tables 17-20. The section below outlines principal
assumptions underlying the economic analysis.
(a) Project Life. LBOD-Stage I would have an expected life of 40 years;
all the equipment is assumed to be replaced during the project
according to the following estimates of useful life (in years), which
reflect recent Pakistani experience: TWs, 15; turbine pumps, 8;
centrifugal pumps, 5; vehicles and other equipment, 10.
(b) Opportunity Cost of Capital (OCC). For purposes of discounting
project cost and benefits, an OCC of 10% is used in the analysis.
(c) Prices. For internationally traded commodities, prices are derived
from Bank-projected world market prices (1984-95) expressed in
mid-1984 currency values. Appropriate adjustments are made for
freight, handling, and processing costs. Because Pakistan is
expected to export cotton and wheat, and to be self-sufficient in
sugar, export parity prices are used for these items. Import parity
prices are used for oilseeds and fertilizer, because these items



-43-
would probably be imported for the foreseeable future. Prices of
nontraded goods and services are expressed in border rupees through
the application of estimated conversion factors for their principal
categories that account for opportunity costs and local taxes and
duties. Unskilled labor is assumed to have an opportunity cost
estimated at 80% of the market wage rate during periods of low
agricultural labor demand (about six months). The economic price
of electricity assumes that TW power dpmand would be supplied by
base-load generation, which is a combination of steady hydroelectric
and low-cost thermal plants. Thus, the economic cost for the
project's power demand is assumed to lie between the estimated
off-peak base-load cost and peak thermal costs, resulting in a power
cost that is about 452 above the current financial tariff. Input and
output prices, including the conversion factors, used in the economic
analysis are summarized in Annex 7, Table 5.
(d) Proiect Costs. All project investment costs (inclusive of physical
contingencies), except portions of the item indicated below, are
included in the economic analysis and are adjusted according to the
price assumptions outlined under item (c). The excluded item refers
to project's expenditures on the spinal drain incurred before comple-
tion of the appraisal, comprising about 15% of Stage I costs in
mid-1984 prices, which are treated as justified sunk costs. Although
the collective drainage system is expected eventually to serve a
larger area, given the uncertain timing and required incremental
capacity of future stages, the full economic costs of this
infrastructure are borne by LBOD-Stage I. These common costs are
included in the project's overall ERR estimate, but are not included
in the separate estimates of economic rate of return for subareas,
because of the difficulty in apportioning collective costs to each
subarea. Due to the limited life of project equipment, economic
costs include the required reinvestment costs as indicated under item
(a); incremental OEM costs, based on the estimated economic price of
energy, are also included. The above economic costs and their main
components (capital and OEM) for each subarea and for the overall
Stage-I project are shown in Annex 7, Tables 18-20.
(e) Benefits and Phasing. Primary project benefits are estimated almost
entirely (99%) on the basis of increased irrigated cropped area and
yields, which are the result of the provision of drainage and addi-
tional water supplies. The phasing of these benefits accounts for
the project's 8-year implementation period and the phasing of expen-
ditures for project components. Generally, "with" the project,
yields and cropping intensities are expected to materialize shortly
after completion of works in the improved area, with a relatively
rapid but reasonable build-up during the first 5 years to reflect
effects of lowered watertables and the provision of additional water
supplies. Thereafter (or about 1995), for most of the project area
a relatively gradual continuation in the build-up of benefits is
anticipated, until maximum development production levels are reached
(about year 2010). Since a portion of the water supplies made avail-
able by the project-financed irrigation works (para 3.17) would also
benefit other areas adjacent to the Stage I area, these relatively
small and deferred benefits (and associated costs) have been included
in the economic analysis of overall Stage I (Annex 7, Table 20).



-44-
Such estimates are only illustrative and do not alter substantially
the project's ERR. The remaining 1Z of primary economic benefits
would be due to savings in noncrop stormwater damage and in O&M costs
for buildings and roads. The basis for these latter benefits is
discussed in para 7.06. Finally, the project's secondary benefits
from project-induced economic activity in the region are discussed in
paras 7.07-7.08 and the environmental effects are covered in paras
7.09-7.12; these benefits, however, are excluded from the economic
analysis.
Other Primary Benefits
7.06    Tangible benefits would be derived from a reduction in stormwater and
watertable (noncrop) damage. About half of the noncrop stormwater damage
that would occur without the project because of the duration of flooding
would be prevented as a result of adequate watertable control and by direct
surface disposal. At full project development, these noncrop benefits are
estimated to total about Rs 24 M per annum. Controlling the watertable would
also reduce the long-term effects of inadequate subsoil drainage on buildings
and roads. At full development, annual savings (nearly Rs 20 M) in main-
tenance costs of roads, buildings, and utility lines would be realized. This
figure assumes that only 5% of current (low) maintenance public expenditures
on the above items would be saved. Unquantifiable benefits from both surface
and subsurface drainage would also improve health and sanitation conditions.
The surface drainage system could be used for foul-water disposal on a larger
scale, although some pretreatment would be desirable. A limited extension of
rural electrification (about 20% of project incremental load) would also
benefit the project area. These indirect project benefits would contribute
to improving the living standards of the predominantly rural population.
Secondary Economic Impact
7.07    Due to its size and scope, LBOD-Stage I would inevitably generate
additional economic effects, not fully captured in the analysis of primary
benefits, in the project area and in other parts of Sind Province. The
project is expected to increase general economic activity by the multiplier
effects of both its capital and recurrent expenditure and by the resultant
expansion in agricultural and, to a lesser extent, from nonagricultural
production and incomes. The analysis of secondary impact shows that, for
each Rs 1.0 M increase in direct value added in the agriculture sector (that
is, the gross value of project production less the cost of goods and services
from other economic sectors), an additional Rs 1.4 M would be generated in
the value added in the provincial economy. About half of this induced expen-
diture would be generated by increased on-farm activity and incomes, and
about half by increased activity and incomes in agro-processing and distribu-
tive trades and other services (including higher capacity utilization rates
of project-related activities through direct and multiplier effects). About
55% of this secondary impact (in terms of total value added) would occur in
the three subareas, benefitting the predominantly low-income rural population
of Sind Province.
7.08    Other secondary benefits include the generation of additional and
remunerative employment opportunities in related nonagricultural activities
(about 120,000 man-years) and of additional provincial revenues (estimated at
about Rs 55 M per annum at full development), plus higher levels of federal



-45-
tax revenues, which constitute the bulk of total revenues collected in the
province. No significant secondary costs have been identified. Since the
secondary benefits do not entirely reflect incremental benefits to the
national economy, they were not included in the economic analysis. Never-
theless, such benefits were estimated in order to illustrate the project's
significant role and regional impact in supporting GOSind's current policy of
promoting regional development in its rural areas. The secondary impact
report providing the detailed assumptions and results is available in the
project file.
Environmental ImDact
7.09    The project would significantly improve the environment of the
perenn.xal canal comands in the project area by reversing or preventing the
deterioration of productive agricultural land through waterlogging and
salinity. Drainage would improve the productive capacity of farmland and
allow rainfall to leach salts to greater depth on uncultivated land, thereby
reducing salinization. The quality of vegetation on this land (although much
is presently barren) is expected to improve appreciably. Surface drainage
and lower watertables not only would minimize flood and high watertable
damage to crops in agricultural areas (and to buildings, roads, structures,
and utility lines throughout the whole of the project area, including urban
areas), but also would reduce standing water, thereby eliminating breeding
grounds for malarial and other mosquitoes and pollution from domestic and
industrial effluents. Water, which supports a varied aquatic life and
provides wintering grounds for migratory birds, would remain in deeper
depressions, such as those left by old river courses in the western part of
Nawabshah. Wildlife protection capability in the area would be strengthened
by the provision of staff training (para 3.20). Offices and quarters for
field staff monitoring envirormental impact in the Wildlife Sanctuary would
be provided under the project (para 5.04). The improvement of both physical
and productive aspects of the environment would contribute significantly to
increased incomes and a better quality of life for the 1.2 million
inhabitants of the project area.
7.10    Areas adjacent to open drains carrying saline effluent generally
would not be affected by such effluent. Although most of the water extracted
by the drainage wells would be highly saline, losses in disposal channels
would be relatively small and would mostly percolate to the watertable.
Where considerable seepage is expected from TW disposal lines, pipe would be
provided to reduce seepage losses. The effect of seepage from the main,
branch, and sub-drains would also be minimized by routing these away from
cultivated land, along command boundaries. Also, the saline baseflow is
likely to be diluted by irrigation losses and in the largest drains, would
be at a depth only slightly above the general level of the watertable.
Baseflow levels in the lower part of the spinal drain would be at or below
watertable depth and, therefore, would have little or no effect on adjacent
areas.
7.11    With adequate safeguards provided in the design of the L2BOD-Stage I
project (para 3.04), the drainage water in DPOD flowing into Shakoor Dhand
would be principally stormwater. Drainage flows from KPOD into the tidal
link connecting to the Arabian Sea should have no effect on the natural
environment, nor should they affect the livelihood of the fishermen in the
area. The stormwater (mainly freshwater) flows to the Shakoor Dhand and to



-46-
GOSind's associated wildlife sanctuary in the area are critical to the
stability of the wetlands areas, which support wildlife and fisheries. Staff
of the Wildlife Sanctuary, trained under the project, would closely monitor
and report any observed ecological changes. The design of the weir con-
structed across the Dhoro Link would regulate the quantity and quality of
drainage flow so that no material change in discharge would occur. The
transboundary flows to and from India are expected to remain as before. The
weir would divert all saline base flows (up to 62.3 cms) into the KPOD, where
it would be partially diluted by intermittent freshwater discharge (up to
28.3 cms) from the Kotri drain before being discharged through the tidal link
to the sea. Bunds would prevent any spillage into the Rann of Kutch or
transboundary flow into India. The bunds would also protect the drain and
adjacent areas of Kotri command from the effects of abnormally high water
levels in the Rann. The baseflow into the tidal creek connecting to the sea
would be small compared with the existing ebb and flow of the tide in this
channel, and therefore would have little effect on estuarine salinities and
fish. The quantity and quality of drainage effluent in the DPOD and KPOD
would be monitored closely by trained staff of the Department of Forestry and
Wildlife during construction and throughout operation to provide early warn-
ing of unforeseen environmental changes. Emphasis would be given to monitor-
ing of fish populations in Chotiari Reservoir and permanent lakes in the
project area, as well as in the outfall area.
7.12    The increased capacity of Chotiari Reservoir would expand the breed-
ing habitat for fish species in the existing Chotiari Lakes, which currently
depend on flood waters for successful breeding, benefitting neighboring
fishing communities comprising an estimated 1,800 active fishermen. Chotiari
Reservoir impoundment would create negligible damage and resettlement costs,
since only a few temporary accommodations for fishermen are located in the
reservoir inundation area.
Project Risks and Sensitivity Tests
7.13    The main project risks involve possible delays in completing the
integrated project works, particularly the irrigation-related investments,
and inadequate O&M of the project's drainage facilities. As stated in para
7.05, the project's economic viability is ensured through a design Lhat
integrates and phases the drainage and irrigation investments. If these
irrigation works are substantially delayed (for example, because of a
shortage in available financing or a decision not to develop the available
irrigation supplies for use in the project area), a major opportunity for
expansion of agricultural production would be lost, and the economic
viability of LBOD-Stage I would be jeopardized. The economic viability of
the Sanghar and Mirpurkhas subareas would be particularly affected, since an
important portion of their benefits is derived from these additional water
supplies (Annex 7, Table 21). To minimize these risks, measures have been
taken to ensure timely completion of such works, including phasing project
expenditures to be consistent with GOSind's planned expenditures in the
current Sixth Five-Year Plan and the upcoming Seventh Plan (FY89-93), and the
provision of necessary external financing for the irrigation component, and
the establishment of a special account to provide timely funds. A reduction
in the scope of irrigation works in LBOD-Stage I would lessen the project's
drainage requirements and thereby would imply overdesign in costs (capital
and O0K) and overestimation of benefits. These works have been given high
priority by GOP, as evidenced by its relatively firm investment decisions,



-47-
allocations, and detailed studies for all three irrigation components,
thereby indicating that these works should proceed as agreed. The scope of
the program of watercourse improvements has been limited to 50% of project
area watercourses, with improvements phased over 8 years, since it would
involve the active participation of farmers (many of whom are tenants) i.
construction activities. Also, project benefits have assumed moderate
improvements in the provision of agricultural support services currently
underway in other programs; in this context, support services are not
expected to constitute a project risk. Risks associated with inadequate O&M
of the project's drainage system, which could contribute to a shortfall in
expected incremental agricultural production, would be minimized by
implementing specific technical, organizational, and financial measures to
overcome such O&M problems as experienced in other drainage projects (para.
5.10). Finally, IMD, supported by extensive technical assistance and a
strengthened monitoring system, would provide a strong management team to
execute necessary project designs and the timely implementation of project
works, in order to minimize risks associated with delay in project comple-
tion.
7.14    Sensitivity tests of the project's economic analysis and its main
underlying determinants were made to assess the impact of the main risks,
should they materialize. These tests (e.g. a lowering of expected yield
increases and cropping intensities, a drop in projected prices of wheat and
cotton, and/or a delay in the build-up of project beaefits) and project
switching values show that IBOD-Stage I can absorb a reasonable amount of
shock and still remain economically viable (Annex 7, Table 21); if overall
benefits decrease by nearly 20%, or costs increase by as much as 22Z, or if
all benefits are delayed by about two years, the project would still meet the
economic viability criteria.  Table 7.1 simuariz.es the switching values for
each subarea and for Stage I overall. The important determinants of project
costs and benefits cited above are unlikely to exceed the above percentages
and are not expected to put the project at risk. Especially in Mirpurkhas,
which shows the lowest economic rate of return (12.2%) and the highest rela-
tive risk, because of serious constraints to agricultural development due to
a high watertable and low cropping intensity, inclusion of all project com-
ponents are essential. As indicated above, main potential risks are being
addressed by specific project actions.
VIII.  AGREENMNTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
8.01    Assurances have been obtained from GOP that:
(a) adequate provisions would be made to complete the spinal drain and
associated structures up to Nawabshah to required capacity by
July 31, 1986, or such other date as GOP and IDA may agree (para
3.03); and
(b) a special account would be set up and operated in accordance with
guidelines agreed by IDA (para 4.07).



-48-
8.02    Assurances would be obtained from GOP and GOSind that:
(a) the water supplies made available by the Nara and Jamrao Canal
remodelling work and the construction of the Chotiari Reservoir would
be fully utilized in LBOD-Stage I and adjoining areas, including the
proposed Makhi-Faresh Project (para 3.17);
(b) necessary resources and funds to execute the project in accordance
with agreed implementation and expenditure schedules would be
provided promptly as required (para 4.06);
(c) (i) WAPDA (South) and GOSind would prepare and maintain separate
project accounts; (ii) IMD would prepare and maintain consolidated
project accounts, including records of the three special subaccounts;
(iii) the project accounts would be audited annually by an independ-
ent auditor satisfactory to IDA; (iv) copies of the unaudited annual
project accounts would be sent to IDA for review no later than six
months after the end of each fiscal year and a certified copy of the
final auditor's report no later than nine months after the end of
each fiscal year; (v) the auditor's report would include, inter alia,
statements that the use of IDA funds advanced for the special account
and against statements of expenditure had been used for the purpose
for which they were provided (para 4.13); and
{d) GOSind would ensure that the Rohri Canal Remodelling progresses at a
rate such that the benefits of such remodelling are available in the
Rohri Canal command area falling within the project area not later
than January 1, 1990, or such other date as IDA may agree, and that
adequate funds would be made available for such works (para 3.14).
8.03    Assurances have been obtained from GOSind that:
Ca) special subaccounts for expenditures by the Departments of Irrigation
and Agriculture, respectively, on canal remodelling and OFWM works,
to be financed by IDA, would be set up and operated according to IDA
guidelines (para 4.07);
(b) training of at least two additional OFWM field teams would be com-
pleted no later than April 1, 1985 (para 5.05);
(c)  (i) the organization of formal WUAs would be a pre-condition to
farmers receiving OFWM assistance, and (ii) GOSind would undertake a
phased program to extend the WUA functions to drainage O&M and make
amendments as necessary no later than July 1, 1986 to the Water
Users' Association Ordinance of 1982, to extend the WUAs' powers and
responsibilities to cover all aspects of drainage, including respon-
sibility for construction and maintenance of farm drains (para 5.06);
(d) provincial O&M staff would be seconded to the project on OEM assign-
ment for at least one year prior to completion of work in a given
subarea (para 5.10);
(e)  adequate and timely funds would be made available annually for actual
full O&M requirements (including capital replacement costs) as deter-
mined by periodic reviews to maintain acceptable O&M standards and



-49-
operational efficiency of the drainage system over the long term
(para 5.13);
(f) GOSind's Department of Planning and Development and IND would prepare
and send to IDA for comment by September 30, 1985 terms of reference
and invitations for proposals from independent local institutions
for project impact evaluation; and a contract would be concluded with
a suitable local institution acceptable to IDA to carry out the
project's evaluation study under terms of reference and conditions
satisfactory to IDA by February 1, 1986. The local institution would
also complete a subcontract with a suitable international institution
by February 1, 1986 to assist in carrying out the evaluation study
and in preparing a project completion report within 6 months of the
close of the project (para 5.15); and
(g)  (i) the status and required actions to achieve irrigation O&M cost
recovery by July 1, 1988, in accordance with covenants previously
obtained, would be reviewed with IDA no later than February 1, 1986;
(ii)(a) about one year after commencement of full operation of the
facilities in each sub-area, to collect drainage charges from the
beneficiaries to cover 25% of the O&M costs of the facilities; (b)
thereafter, at least every two years, increase the drainage charges
so as to recover at least 50% of the O&M costs by July 1, 1995 and
10OZ of the O&M costs by July 1, 2005, provided that the drainage
charges keep pace and are commensurate with the benefits actually
accruing to the beneficiaries; (iii) at least three months before
the initial charge and subsequent increases, to review with TDA the
estimated actual farm benefits against the projected benefits and
proposed charges; and (iv) farmers benefitting from OFWM activities
would be required to contribute all unskilled labor and repay a
portion (25%) of the cash cost of materials and skilled labor over
five years, in accordance with existing agreements (para 6.19).
8.04    Assurances have been obtained from GOSind and WAPDA that:
(a)  they would provide the staff and local consultants required for
project execution on a timely basis and, specifically, WAPDA would
appoint by April 1, 1985 the key IMD staff required for PY1 implemen-
tation (para 5.03);
(b)  WAPDA would complete the appointment and fielding of consultants no
later than June 1, 1985; supervisory consultants, if required, for
OFWH would be appointed by GOSind no later than January 1, 1987, and
arrangements for staff training would be satisfactory to IDA (para
5.08);
(c)  at the completion of construction, and again prior to overall project
handover to GOSind, a joint inspection of the drainage and irrigation
water storage facilities would be made by IMD, GOSind and WAPDA to
identify deficiencies and agree on corrective actions (para 5.10);
and



-50-
(d) during project implementation and thereafter, an independent team
acceptable to IDA would monitor and review at three year intervals
drainage OEM requirements in the LBOD Stage I project area, starting
no later than January 1, 1988 (para 5.11).
8.05    Assurances have been obtained from WAPDA that:
(a) a special subaccount for expenditures on drainage and electrification
works financed by IDA would be set up and operated according to IDA
guidelines (para 4.07);
(b) operational procedures would be developed in greater detail by the
IM0 and a draft O&M manual would be prepared and submitted to IDA for
review and comment no later than January 1, 1988 and sequentially
updated as project components are completed, with a final report no
later than January 1, 1991 (para 5.12); and
(c) IDM would prepare and send to IDA for comment by September 30, 1985
a detailed monitoring and reporting program, and by February 1, 1986
a Memorandum of Understanding would be concluded with WAPDA's M&E
Organization to monitor physical aspects of the project (para 5.15).
8.06    Conditions of Board presentation would be that:
(a) GOP acquire the land required for the first year's civil works to
be financed by IDA (para 3.22); and
(b) the Minister of Finance, as Chairman of Executive Committee of the
National Economic Council (ECNEC), approve, on an anticipatory basis,
the final Umbrella PC-1, including the OFWM component (para 4.06).
8.07    Conditions of credit effectiveness would be:
Ca) ECNEC approval of the final Umbrella PC-1 (para 4.06);
'b) meeting all conditions precedent to the effectiveness of other exter-
nal financing agencies (para 4.06);
(c) the establishment of a special account (para 4.07); and
(d) the establishment of the Project Steering Committee (PSC) and the
Integrated Management Organization (IND) with powers and functions
satisfactory to IDA (para 5.01).
8.08    With the above assurances, the project would be suitable for an IDA
Credit of SDR 147.6 M (US$150 M) on standard terms. The borrower would be
the Islamic Republic of Pakistan.

I/ A series of land classification maps at a scale of 1:50,000 were produced
covering the LBOD-Stage I project area in 16 sheets. The following
classification criteria were used:
Class 1:    land that has no limitations and, therefore, no subclasses.
Class 2s:   land with minor soil limitations in the form of fine medium
subsoil texture, (0-150 cm dominated by group H or H/V).
Class 3s:   land with moderate limitations of unfavourable textures
CL or V groups dominant between 0-150) or high salinity
combined with H or V textural groups.
Class 3sd:  land with one or all of the above limitations and, in addition,
substratum textures in the V group which would require
closer drain spacing.
Class 6ts:  land outside the irrigation system command and with limitations
of uneven topography due to sand deposition. Former river
channels (topographic depressions) may also occur as a minor
part of this unit. Sandy soil textures also introduce a
soil limitation.
Class 6f:   land already flooded or subject to flooding.

ANNEX 4
Page 1
PAKISTAN
LEFT BANK OUTFALL DRAIN STAGE I PROJECT
Procedures for Operation of Special Account
1.       A "Special Accountt (or Imprest Account) would be opened in the
State Bank of Pakistan by GOP.
2.       GOP's Finance Division would authorize GOSind and WAPDA to open
separate subaccounts (Personal Ledger Accounts) in the Treasury for purposes
of receiving funds from the Special Account and making payments for items
eligible for financing from the proceeds of the IDA Credit. GOSind would set
up two separate subaccounts for expenditures on OFWM4 (watercourse improve-
ments and staff costs) and irrigation (Nara and Jamrao Canal remodelling)
works to be financed by IDA and carried out by GOSind's Departments of
Agriculture and Irrigation, respectively. These subaccounts would be
individually administered by the respective Secretaries of these Departments,
under the overall supervision of GOSind's Finance Secretary. The WAPDA
subaccount would be established for expenditures for drainage (KPOD and DPOD)
and electrification works to be financed by IDA and would be administered by
the Chief Engineer, IMO. In order to facilitate the operation of the subac-
counts, the 110 would provide overall coordination, such as consolidating the
status of the three subaccounts in close collaboration with the three
entities and GOSind's Finance Secretary.
3.       Promptly after the Effective Date of the Development Credit Agree-
ment, and at the request of the Borrower, IDA wc'zld withdraw rupees (Rs) 75
million (M) equivalent from the Credit Account for deposit in the Special
Account. Based on the project requirements and request from the participat-
ing entities immediately following Credit effectiveness, IMO would instruct
the State Bank of Pakistan to transfer the following initial amounts to two
subaccounts Rs 1.5 M for OEUM and Rs 40 M for drainage and electrification
works; it is expected that IMO would instruct the State Bank to transfer an
initial Rs 3.1 M for the irrigation works in the beginning of FY86/87 in
accordance with the project's expenditure schedule. Subsequent to the ini-
tial deposits, upon receipt of withdrawal applications from GOSind's Depart-
ments of Agriculture and of Irrigation and WAPDA, and under the overall
coordination of IMO, IDA would further withdraw from the Credit Account to
deposit and maintain in the Special Account a level of rupees for payment of
eligible expenditures required by the three agencies for a consecutive
four-month period. IMO would play an important coordinating role to ensure



-68-
ANNEX 4
Page 2
that a low utilization rate of one subaccount does not jeopardize the funds
made available to and required by another subaccount.
4.      Payments from the subaccounts would be exclusively for approved
eligible expenditures stated under para. 2. All withdrawals from GOSind's
subaccounts by the Secretaries of Agriculture and Irrigation would be in
accordance with the procedures prescribed by GOSind for operation of Personal
Ledger Accounts. Withdrawals from the WAPDA subaccount by the Chief
Engineer, IMO, would be in accordance with procedures prescribed by GOP.
Copies of these procedures would be forwarded to IDA. Checks to withdraw
funds from a subaccount would be signed by the respective Secretary/Chief
Engineer and co-signed by an appropriate officer designated by GOSind/WAPDA.
All documents substantiating any payment made from the subaccount would be
retained by the Departments of Agriculture and of Irrigation and WAPDA.
5.       On the basis of monthly statements of expenditures, certified by
the supervisory consulting team leader, the Department of Agriculture would
prepare reimbursement applications for signature by the authorized official
and submit them to IDA (through the Resident Mission, Islamabad); based on
full documentation of other project expenditures being financed under the
Credit and implemented by WAPDA and the Department of Irrigation, IMD would
coordinate the submission of reimburserment applications to IDA (also through
the Resident Mission). The payment instructions in the applications should
clearly state the name and number of the subaccount to ensure proper transfer
of funds. IDA would withdraw from the Credit Account the reimbursable
amounts and remit them to the Special Account.
6.       If for any reason IDA should disallow or reduce a claim submitted by
GOSind's Departments of Agriculture and of Irrigation and WAPDA, GOSind o-
WAPDA, as applicable, would be required to make up the amount in the sub-
account.
7.       The subaccounts would be audited in accordance with the provisions
of Section 4.01 of the Development Credit Agreement.
8.       No further deposit in the Special Account/subaccounts would be made
when the total amount withdrawn has reached the equivalent of SDR 119.2 M
(US$121.2 M).
9.       GOP, GOSind, or WAPDA, as required, would obtain from the State Bank
of Pakistan and furnish to IDA any information relating to the Special
Account and subaccounts as IDA may reasonably request.

LEFT BANK OUTFALL DRAIN STAGE I PROJECT
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE
A. Terms of Reference for Main Consulting Services
Introduction
1.      IMO and GOSind's Irrigation Department would require technical assis-
tance principally in planning, designing, and implementing the lower outfall
system (including the KPOD/DPOD bifurcation structure and tidal link), sub-
surface drainage (TW fields, tile drains, and interceptor drains), and
irrigation components of the project. The consultants also would assist IMO
and the Department in setting up and implementing satisfactory procedures
for O&M. The consultants would be an internationally selected firm or con-
sortium in association with a suitable local firm and would be employed in
accordance with World Bank Guidelines for the Use of Consultants, August
1981. It is expected that the consultants would be posted no later than July
1, 1985.
ScoDe of Work
2.      The scope of work is as outlined in the Staff Appraisal Report (para
3.02).
Consulting Services
3.      Services to be provided by the consultants would include but not be
limited to assistance in:
(a) Collection and analyses of data;
(b) Preparation of work plans and PERT chart for the implementation
of all project components;
(c) Investigation, design, layout, preparation of specifications and
engineer's cost estimates, and supervision of construction of
the surface and subsurface drainage systems and of Nara/Jamrao
canal remodelling work and Chotiari Reservoir;
(d) Establisbment of criteria and procedures for technical work and
construction specifications;
(e) Certification of technical acceptability of completed designs
and specification drawings;
(f) Preparation of tender documents for civil works and evaluation
of bids in accordance with cofinanciers' requirements;



ANNEX 6
-74-                     Page 2
(g) Monitoring of completed works for potential problems and pos-
sible improvement in design;
(h) Technical advice on specific subsurface drainage design require-
ments resulting from different aquifer and soil conditions;
(i) Imparting to contractors understanding of the construction
specifications, inspection requirements, and progress reporting
requirements;
(j) On-the-job training and guidance in materials inspection and
installation techniques used for the construction of the subsur-
face tile drainage system and TW design and installation;
(k) Setting up monitoring systems for selected physical parameters
and monitoring the project's physical and financial progress;
and advising as appropriate GOSIND's Planning and Development
Department in: finalizing TOR and invitations for proposals from
suitable independent local institutions for the project impact
evaluation study; and in executing such study, particularly
during critical phases of the work plan.
'.) Reviewing inspection reports and preparing monthly summaries
of drain inspection problems/remedies;
(m) Advising IMO and GOSind's Irrigation Department on O&M proce-
dures and in preparing the O&M manual;
(n) Certifying the completeness and acceptability of completed
works; and
Co) assisting with the preparation of quarterly and comprehensive
annual reports.
Duty Station and DisciDlines Required
4.      The consultants would be stationed principally at the IND's main
office in Hyderabad; however, individual team members may occasionally be
posted to satellite offices of project directors for short periods of time.
The following expertise would be required:

B. Terms of Reference for OFWM Assistance
Introduction
1.      GOSind will implement the OEWM component under the project with its
own staff of the Department of Agriculture during the first project year.
Provision has been made, however, for the appointment of local supervisory
consultants from January 1, 1987 to assist the Department of Agriculture
staff if necessary to meet the project's targets in watercourse improvement
and adequate supervision of project works.
Description of Work
2.      The team of local supervisory consultants would provide assistance to
GOSind's OFWM Directorate in the overall coordination and management of OFIM
wor'k.l/ The te-a would report to the director of the Directorate and be
under his general supervision.
Scope of Work
3.      The consulting team would provide the following services:
(a) review plans and designs for OFWM civil works to assure
compliance with agreed criteria and standards;
(b)  review technicai adequacy of revised standards proposed by the
Directorate;
.J Terms and conditions of agreement and experience and qualifications of
consulting personnel shall be satisfactory to IDA.



ANNEX 6
-76-                     Page 4
(c) verify statements of expenditures for procured materials;
(d) check structures during installation and all completed civil
works to assure that such works meet the agreed criteria and
standards of eligibility for IDA financing;
(e) notify OFWM Director of compliance or non-compliance of comp-
leted civil works with agreed criteria and standards;
(f) prepare reimbursement applications to IDA for the Directorate,
and verification of quantities and quality;
(g) assist the Directorate in preparing annual vork plans and
progress and completion reports, as well as in updating the
project work plan; and
(h) monitor, review, and assist in procurement activities.
4.      Following completion of civil works by the GOSind's OFWM Directorate,
the consultants would check all renovated watercourses to verify quantity
and quality of structures and sections. In addition, they would check
adequacy of precision land levelling of the demonstration plot. Deficiencies
noted would be reported to the Directorate at least 45 days prior to
certification of completion by the Directorate.
5.      The consultants would assist in preparing progress and completion
reports, annual and updated project work plans, and budget control in
accordance with directives from the Director; they would give particular
attention to and assistance in keeping OFWM work on schedule and in
resolving constraints that otherwise would delay the work.
Duty Station and Disciplines Required
6.      The consultants would be stationed in Hyderabad in close proximity
to GOSind's OFWM Directorate. It is expected that the consultants would be
appointed no later than January 1, 1987. The following expertise would
be provided:
Profession                               No.      Nan/Months
Team Leader, Engineer (full-time)         3          174
Accountant                                3           45
Sub-engineer (part-time)                  3           45
Total months                                264
7.      The local consulting firm's fee would include all personnel costs
(salaries, related expenses, and allowances); overhead; transport, office
and survey equipment; transportation costs; rent of office facilities
and utilities; and clerical support and drivers. In addition, the firm
would supply limited back-up staff support, services, and technical
assistance from its headquarters staff as required.

PAKISTAN
LEFT BANK OUTFALL DRAIN STAGE I PROJECT
Outline of Terms of Reference for
Monitoring and Evaluation Component
Introduction
1.      The project's monitoring and evaluation (M&E) activities (paras.
3.21, 5.14, 5.15) would establish and implement an effective and timely
information system with the following main objectives:
(a) to report the physical and financial progress in implementation of
project activities that would provide an early warning of bottlenecks
and guide appropriate and coordinated corrective actions by the
implementing agencies and cofinanciers;
(b) to monitor the main physical parameters that may affect the project's
physical impact, therefore enabling appropriate and timely corrective
actions should any problems and/or sub-optimal conditions materialize
during or after project implementation;
Cc) to assess the project's socio-economic impact on beneficiaries'
agricultural production and income levels, and to a lesser extent, on
the regional economy; it is expected that this independent evaluation
study would provide information that could be used to monitor benefit
build-up, to maximize project benefits, to guide implementation of
drainage charge agreements, and to learn more systematically the
lessons for planning subsequent stages of LBOD and other similar
drainage projects.
The M&E component would comprise three inter-related and coordinated
activities-project progress reporting; monitoring physical parameters; and
socio-economic impact evaluation. The terms of reference (TOR) for each
activity are outlined below. Further elaboration on the TOR and detailed
plans for implementing these activities and their linkages, including coor-
dination in collection and use of information, would be prepared during the
early stages of project execution in accordance with the following framework
and the Bank's Guidelines (A Handbook on Monitoring and Evaluation of
Agriculture and Rural Development Proiects. World Bank. 1981).



-99-
ANNEX 8
Page 2
A.  MonitorinR of Proiect Implementation
2.      Under the overall coordination of the IMO, reporting on project
implementation would include progress in: (a) the number of TVs and elec-
trification works installed and dates of installation, TW performance (until
these are transferred to GOSind), length of main drains completed and canals
remodelled, and the number of watercourses improved; (b) implementation and
effectiveness of scavenger wells, TWs, interceptor and surface drains; (c)
the posting and training of project staff; (d) the procurement of equipment,
civil works, and consultants; (e) administrative and institutional arrange-
ments and coordination; and (f) financial expenditure by project component
and by budget bead, including operation of the project's special
sub-accounts.
3.      The reporting format would emphasize timely intervention by project
management for early resolution of potential bottlenecks. To simplify
reporting requirements of project management to the Government and cofinan-
ciers and to increase the effectiveness of regular project reviews, IMO,
assisted by project consultants, would prepare a reporting program and desig-
nate staff responsibilities for review by IDA by February 1, 1986. There-
after, IMO would prepare annual progress reports for submission to Government
and the cofinanciers by February 1 of each following year.
B. Monitoring ProRram of Physical Parameters
4.      WAPDA's M&E Organization, supported by concerned implementing divi-
sions, would monitor project works and their main hydrological effects. The
main parameters monitored would include: (a) watertable depths throughout the
project area; (b) water balance (including canal supplies, rainfall,
evapotranspiration, TW and tile drain pumpage, reuse, and drain outflows);
Cc) salt balance and incoming sediment in the canals; (d) performance of TWs
and horizontal drain pump stations (including well/drain hydraulics, O&M, and
water quality); (e) performance of the disposal system, including subdrains
and branch drains as well as the outfalls to Shakoor Dhand and the Arabian
Sea.
5.      The following data collection programs would be carried out:  (a) for
vatertable depth monitoring, the existing network of observation wells would
be augmented by a suitable number of piezometers installed by IM and all
wells would be monitored twice yearly; (b) a number of meteorological sta-
tions and recording raingauges would be installed by 1MO to complement exist-
ing facilities; (c) canal flows into and out of the subareas would be
monitored at various sites by a regular canal discharge measurement program;
(d) drain discharges would be monitored at a number of sites, with recording
and non-recording gauges (installed by IMO); te) crest stage recorders would
be installed to record depth of flooding above sample inlets and flood levels
at critical points within the disposal system; (f) TW and horizontal draiu
performance would be monitored recording actual pumping, power consumption
and breakdowns; (g) the special TV studies would be undertaken as part of
project implementation; (h) performance of the tidal outfall would be
monitored by a permanent tidegauge to be installed in Shah Samando Creek,
together with EC recorders in the tidal linkJKPOD system.



-100-
ANNEX 8
Page 3
6.      The M&E team would prepare quarterly reports on vell performance and
annual reports to summarize and analyze data collected. The reports would
recomend required action(s) to correct identified problems and/or optimize
the project's physical (hydrological) operation. Such reports would be
submitted to IMO and GOSind's Department of Irrigation no later than March 1
of each year, beginning in 1987. To provide a complete record of project
operation and performance, the monitoring reports would be closely coor-
dinated with the annual project progress reports work prepared by IMO.
7.      A detailed monitoring program for the above activities vould be
prepared by WAPDA's M&E organization, including a work schedule and organiza-
tion, including designated personnel and costs. This program would form the
basis of a memorandum of understanding to be reached between IMO and WAPDA's
M&E Unit to be completed by no later than February 1, 1986. This memorandum
would be sent to IDA and the other participating cofinanciers for review and
comment.
C. Socio-Economic Impact Evaluation Study
8.      Key Indicators.  The impact evaluation study would collect and
analyze data on the following key indicators: crop yields, cropped area and
increases in cropping intensities; cropping patterns; land use, particularly
land abandonment; crop and livestock production; farm incomes of
beneficiaries, including assessment of their ability to meet agreed project
cost recovery provisions; distribution of project direct benefits, with
emphasis on assessing the impact on tenants and other low income groups; farm
employment levels; secondary economic impacts; effectiveness of WUA's in
providing adequate O&M of improved watercourses and field drains; and other
relevant and unforeseen project impacts that may emerge.
9.       Approach.  The evaluation should be as simple and focused as pos-
sible, and should make maximum use of existing information and data including
available primary and secondary data sources, information generated during
project preparation and appraisal, satellite imagery data, and aerial
photography (where appropriate). This approach should facilitate comparison
of forecasted and estimated actual changes of key parameters (e.g.,
depth-to-watertable, cropping intensities, farm incomes) according to repre-
sentative subareas. Collection, including field survey design and analysis
of data should seek to establish project benefits and estimate the extent to
which the main production and income incremental benefits (e.g., difference
between "with" and "without" project scenarios) can be attributed to direct
project actions or to non-project factors. The evaluation study should: (a)
adopt appropriate and cost-effective data collection and analytical
methodologies, including the use of representative farm models and "control"
areas and farmers for establishing reasonable "without" project scenarios;
(b) carry out selected studies on topics cited in para. 8 in order to monitor
the accretion of project benefits; and (c) establish key relationships (e.g.,
depth-to- watertable and crop yields) in order to assess project impact on
production and farm income levels, including farmer's capacity to meet the
project's scheduled cost recovery provisions as illustrated in the farm
models.



-101-
ANNEX 8
Page 4
10.      Schedule of Main Activities.   The impact evaluation study would be
implemented in accordance with the following schedule: invitation of
detailed proposals - September 30, 1985; selection of consultants - February
1, 1986; detailed study design - first half of 1986; baseline survey -
1986/87; intermediate surveys, progress reports, and selected studies on key
topics in order to monitor build-up of project benefits - 1988-1992; interim
evaluation report, including contribution to project completion report - end
1993; post-project surveys, progress reports and final evaluation report -
mid-1997, or about four years after the completion of overall project works.
It is expected that the institution selected to implement the study would
submit (by April 1 of each year) to GOSind, IMO, PSC, and the participating
cofinanciers an annual progress report on the main evaluation activities,
problems, and findings for the period under review, and a summary of planned
eraluation activities and staffing arrangements for the following year. The
first report, which should include a detailed implementation plan for the
overall evaluation study, should be submitted to the concerned entities for
their review no later than April 1, 1986. The selected institution also
vould assist GOSind in preparing the project completion report to be sub-
mitted to the cofinanciers within 6 months following the close of the
project.
11.      Implementation Arran2ements.  The evaluation study would be carried
out as a separate project activity by an independent local institution with
the assistance of international experts. Based on the study requirements and
available funding, it is estimated that such experts would: (a) be composed
of an agricultural economist/farm management specialist, an agronomist, a
statistician, and other experts as required (e.g. sociologist); and
(b) provide overall assistance of about 70 man-months over the 11-year study
period. To facilitate coordination among the various participating agencies
in the full utilization of the project's overall information system, par-
ticularly during the early stages of project implementation, the monitoring
data collected by IMO and the WAPDA M&E Unit would be provided to the evalua-
tion agency for the impact evaluation study. GOSind's Planning aud Develop-
ment Department, the study's executing agency, would assist wherever possible
in ensuring smooth and timely coordination and execution of study activities.
Where appropriate and necessary, the PSC would also intervene to ensure that
the study objectives are fulfilled. The participating cofinanciers have
agreed that IDA would administer the ?&E component on behalf of the agencies
providing the funding--CIDA, ODA, and the SWISS. In this capacity, and to
facilitate smooth implementation, IDA would coordinate their joint participa-
tion in this project component.
